0	compressed disks are actually "logical" disks.	<br>you might want to look in windows FAQ for this one, but here is my best<br>explanation.  But I can't guarantee that I'm not way off base...<br><br>The permenant swap file is read/written to by windows by talking<br>directly to the hard disk controller card.  The controller card must<br>use the protocal set up by western digital (or something like that).<br>Windows creates a file called spart.par in your windows directory that<br>points to that file.  It then uses the physical information about your<br>disk to index to information in that file.<br><br>compressed disks are actually "logical" disks.  These disks have different<br>characteristics than the actual physical disk.  Furthermore, the information<br>on the compressed disks must be uncompressed before it is used.  (i.e it must<br>go through the decompression program that traps disk reads at the operating<br>system level or the BIOS level).  Because of this "inbetween" program, windows<br>cannot use direct methods to read from the "logical" disk.<br><br>a permenant swap file is only there to "reserve" an area of the disk that<br>windows can use and to block that space from DOS.  Windows would theoretically<br>not even have to access the file from DOS to use that disk space. (I don't<br>know if it does or doesn't...but it checks for it somewhere everytime you<br>boot windows.)<br><br>a temporary swap file is just a normal DOS file that is accessed by windows<br>via DOS and the BIOS.  If a disk compression program or other TSR is loaded<br>the file access must go through DOS...TSR'S (disk compression)...and BIOS in<br>order to be access. (i.e. NEVER USE A TEMPORARY SWAP FILE...NEVER)<br><br>more on permenent swap files...<br><br>i'm sure everyone who has an uncompressed part of their compressed hard disk<br>has seen the message "you have selected a swap file greater than the suggested<br>size...windows will only use the size suggested...do you wan't to create this<br>swap file anyway" or something like that.<br><br>well, a friend of mine (ROBERT) called microsoft and asked them what and why.<br>what they said is that windows checks the amount of free disk space and<br>divides that number by 2.  Then it checks for the largest contiguous block<br>of free disk space.  Windows then suggests the smaller of the two numbers.<br><br>They also said that under absolutely no circumstances...NONE!...will windows<br>uses a swap file larger than the suggested size.  Well...that's what he <br>said!<br><br>I call bull@#$#.  If this is true why does windows report the memory is<br>available to me if it's not going to use it?<br><br>any takers?<br><br>James

0	****************************************************************************<br><br>Borland has a product called Paradox Engine that does just what you want.	On my own I was able to find out about Codebase, Accsys, and Q+E. Codebase<br>and Accsys are C libraries without SQL. Q+E is a Windows application<br>that can be communicated through Windows DDE calls, where you send a SQL<br>string and receive the results.<br><br>Most people wrote to tell me about the Paradox Engine from Borland. Other <br>products mentioned were: Microsoft's ODBC, Accsys, Quadbase, Codebase,<br>RBASE, and Q+E. Only ODBC, Quadbase, RBASE, and Q+E have SQL.<br><br>For myself, I decided on Codebase, mostly because it has an ANSI C<br>portability version that runs under DOS, UNIX, etc, and includes the<br>source code, and portability is important in my application. It works<br>with DBASE, FoxPro, or Clipper files.<br><br>However, I feel that Microsoft's ODBC looks very promising.<br>It's mostly a formalisation of building and submitting SQL queries, and<br>formatting query results. As the responses say, it's available via ftp,<br>except it's effectively unusuable without two manuals:<br>the "ODBC Programmer's Reference" and the "ODBC SDK Guide". Moreover,<br>ODBC doesn't actually interpret SQL and liase with databases, that's <br>up to drivers that should be provided by database manufacturers. Also,<br>it's Windows only (it's actually an extension to the Windows SDK). <br>Nevertheless, it's a start at a SQL interface standard, and should<br>make life interesting in the future.<br><br>Here's my original post, followed by the responses, separated by <br>a line of asterisks (*).<br><br><br>****************************************************************************<br><br>Borland has a product called Paradox Engine that does just what you want. <br>The current version is 3.0, which is fully compatible with (ack) Paradox<br>4.0.  (Why the versions are different, I don't know. PD Engine 2.0 was<br>compatible with Pdox 3.0 and 3.5...).  It consists of a rather broad<br>library of functions for accesing database files from both Pascal (I think<br>turbo Pascal 5.5 and up) and C (I think either Microsoft or Borland).<br><br>Now.. reading the box... it's "Borland Paradox Engine & Database<br>Frameworks".  Works with any Borland C/C++, Mocrosoft C 6.01 or later,<br>Turbo C++ for Windows, Turbo Pascal 6.0 or later, TP for Windows 1.0 or<br>later.  For Framework applications, BC++ 3.0 or later or MS C/C++ 7.0. It<br>does have support for object useage...<br><br>I haven't had a real chance to really use it myself... but it looks fairly<br>complete... I'm planning to use it this summer.<br>-Rick<br>-- <br>+--------------------------------------------------------------------------+<br>+--------------------------------------------------------------------------+<br><br>****************************************************************************<br><br>Borland has a product which is called Paradox Engine. <br>I do not know about MS.<br><br>The paradox engine I know very well, and it definitiely<br>not a replacement for SQL, but it will enable you to<br>save and restore records, has locking, et al. 1	The controller moves a sector to some buffer memory on the<br>controller card, then the CPU moves the data from the buffer to main<br>memory where it's needed.	IDE does not do DMA.  This is because it's like te PC AT hard disk<br>interface.  The controller moves a sector to some buffer memory on the<br>controller card, then the CPU moves the data from the buffer to main<br>memory where it's needed.  If IDE doesn't work this way, then it's not<br>compatible.<br><br>Heck, if IDE used DMA, then you'd hear all kinds of complaints about<br>ISA bus systems with more than 16M RAM not working with their IDE<br>drives.  16M is the DMA addressing limit of the ISA bus, and if IDE<br>did DMA there would be trouble.<br><br>(BTW, there are DMA-enabling signals in the IDE cable spec, but the<br>last report I heard was that they are never implemented, because it<br>would require a different kind of IDE adapter and different drivers.)

0	And, as you can see, even the regular .PFB files have *some* compression.	<br>This isn't entirely true. It is true that TrueType fonts are<br>larger than their ATM counterparts, but ATM fonts *do* get minimal<br>compression. Running Stacker 3.0 report generator, I get:<br><br>  File Type:      Compression Ratio:<br>  -------------   ------------------<br>  *.TTF (TrueType)     1.4:1.0<br>  *.PFB (ATM)          1.1:1.0<br>  *.PFM (ATM Metric)  11.8:1.0<br><br>Although the metric files are small, they compress quite largely.<br>And, as you can see, even the regular .PFB files have *some* compression.<br><br>So, doing the math on one such comparitive font file:<br><br>TTF Times = 83260 bytes/1.4 = 59471 bytes (compressed)<br>PFB Times = 51549 bytes/1.1 = 46862 bytes (Compressed)<br><br>You still win out, even if the ATM Times font *isn't* compressed.<br><br>Your mileage may vary depending on compression program.<br>
1	The only way to gain advantage with a VLB IDE is to hook it up to<br>a caching controller.	<br><br><br><br>Yeah, there is absolutely no use for VLB except for video graphics.<br>And no IDE could possibly take advantage the VLB, because it runs at<br>8 Mhz and 16 bits. Do people forget that the IDE was specifically<br>designed to interface directly with the AT ISA bus? We've seen<br>IDEs come out for EISA, XT ISA, and now even MCA, but at all times<br>it was a 16 bit standard, running at somewhere near 8-10 Mhz. When<br>you run an IDE off of the VLB, there's no way that you're running it<br>at 33 Mhz, it would burn up. Of course same goes for SCSI, ESDI, whatever,<br>none of them run at CPU speed.<br><br>The only way to gain advantage with a VLB IDE is to hook it up to<br>a caching controller. I suspect it would be much, much better to<br>get a software disk cache instead, since you get write-caching as well.<br><br><br>I've seen some Fuji IDE drives going as high as 1G.

1	The short answer is no, don't accept these<br>"imperfections" as "normal" because for the premium one pays for the<br>larger screen monitors, one has a right to expect higher quality, and<br>if you're persistent, you'll receive it.<br><br>	<br>I can't speak for MAG Innovision but as far as NEC is concerned, they are<br>adjusted to "factory-spec" before leaving the central USA distribution point<br>(MA) for the eastern Canadian market. Now, here's the key: NEC *knows* that<br>the >=15" monitors' adjustments are very sensitive to shipping over<br>distances (with all the bumps, rough handling, and such) and therefore<br>*expect* and (at least in theory) *require* that the local *distributor*<br>(not dealer) have it adjusted at the *local* service centre (Bull HN<br>Information Systems in Toronto) before sale. This is the correct and<br>probably only way of having any chance of receiving a "perfect" monitor<br>(the definition of "perfect" seems to depend on how picky one is, in my<br>case quite :).<br><br><br>I, for one, was not willing to accept what I considered a substandard<br>monitor and, after two months of wrangling and direct contact with NEC,<br>finally received a satisfactorily adjusted monitor (not perfect, IMHO,<br>but a major improvement over what the dealer and distributor were trying<br>convince me was "normal"). The short answer is no, don't accept these<br>"imperfections" as "normal" because for the premium one pays for the<br>larger screen monitors, one has a right to expect higher quality, and<br>if you're persistent, you'll receive it.<br><br>

0	The default is 4.	I think the support droid was malfunctioning and confused the disk space<br>limit with the virtual address space limit.  As far as the disk is<br>concerned, you are limited only by the amount of contiguous free space.<br>The limit that causes the message, "Windows will only use ...," is the<br>amount of virtual address space that the Virtual Memory Manager will<br>create, and this is a function of the amount of free memory that you have<br>when Windows starts.<br><br>In the [386enh] section of SYSTEM.INI, you can specify a parameter:<br><br> PageOverCommit=multiplier<br><br>The following description is from the Windows Resource Kit:<br><br> This entry specifies the multiplier what determines the amount of<br> linear address space the VMM will create for the system, which is<br> computed by rounding up the amount of available physical memory to<br> the nearest 4 MB and then multiplying that value by the value<br> specified for PageOverCommit=.  Increasing this value increases the<br> amount of available linear address space, causing the size of data<br> structures to increase.  This also increases paging activity<br> proportionately and can slow down the system.  You can specify a<br> value between 1 and 20.  The default is 4.  To change this entry,<br> you must edit SYSTEM.INI.<br>

0	It is very easy to use.	1. WORKSPACES 1.10 (wspace.zip)<br>                                This was the smallest and simplest<br>   of the workspace managers that I found.  It is very easy to use.<br>   It displays a small window, containing 6 buttons, plus an extra<br>   button for configuration purposes.  One annoying feature was the<br>   title window that is first presented when it is run - you must press<br>   a key (not a mouse button!) for the thing to go away.  Also, it would<br>   have been nice if there was an "ALWAYS ON TOP" setting for the little<br>   window containing the workspace buttons.  Maybe some user-specified<br>   strings on the buttons instead of the numbers one to six might be<br>   a nice feature.  The simplicity and ease of use of this workspace<br>   manager makes it an attractive package.<br><br>2. WORKSHIFT 1.6 (wrksft16.zip)<br>                               While this workspace manager offered a<br>   few features that WORKSPACES 1.10 lacked, it was quite time consuming<br>   to set things up.  With WORKSPACES 1.10, all but the first workspace<br>   is initially empty.  With WORKSHIFT 1.6, you need to take "snapshots"<br>   of how you want each of your workspaces to look like - i.e. what <br>   applications they contain.  Also, the main window is quite large,<br>   but this does allow you to have a small view of what is in each workspace.<br>   With WORKSPACES 1.10, there was no facility for viewing what was in<br>   a workspace without switching to it.  WORKSHIFT 1.6 provides this<br>   viewing functionality which is quite useful.<br><br>3. BIGDESK 2.30 and BACKMENU (backdesk.zip)<br>                            This is an interesting package, which contains<br>   a few other goodies as well as a workspace manager.  Other goodies<br>   include "Backmenu", which provides a pop-up root menu when you<br>   press a certain mouse button - just like in X-Windows.  The menu<br>   is totally configurable, offering unlimited depth of cascading menus,<br>   which is provides quite handy access to applications.  You could<br>   say it is a menu-based alternative to the program manager.  Also<br>   bundled in "backdesk.zip" is a program called "WRUN", which allows<br>   you to run windows applications from a DOS shell under WINDOWS<br>   instead of using the File Manager to run applications.  <br>   The actual workspace manager is called BIGDESK 2.30.  BIGDESK works quite<br>   differently to the other two workspace managers in that it<br>   doesn't provide a certain number of disjoint and separate workspaces.<br>   In fact, it basically enlarges your desktop by a configurable amount,<br>   and you choose which region of the desktop you want your screen to<br>   focus in on.  This means you can have windows overlapping between different<br>   viewing regions, unlike the first two workspace managers in which<br>   each workspace was isolated from the other one.  The BIGDESK control window<br>   allows to to move windows around your enlarged desktop.  Basically<br>   the control window provides a small scale view of your viewing area<br>   while your actual computer screen provides a large scale or blow-up<br>   of a particular section.

0	TT<br>allows for much more flexibility in this area as well.	TrueType is a much richer and more robust hinting language than Type 1 (the hinting<br>of diagonals for instance, which TT does and Type 1 cannot). If you check out the<br>Windows 3.1 core fonts (Times, Arial, Courier, Symbol, Wingdings) you'll see what can<br>be done with TT. There are other fonts out there that have been hand-hinted and not<br>just converted. Some of the Microsoft TT Font Pack 2 fonts are hinted very well.<br><br><br>Again, not true. The characters in a TT or Type 1 font depend on the maker. If someone<br>converts a Type 1 font to TT they'll only get the characters in the font of orgin. TT<br>allows for much more flexibility in this area as well. You can have thousands of glyphs<br>in a TT font file (Mac/PS/Windows/Unicode char set) and use the characters appropriate<br>for the particular platform you are running the font on. For instance, all or our fontpack<br>2 TT fonts have the Mac/Windows char set in them. The metrics of the fonts are such that<br>if the font is brought over to the mac the user will have access to the full mac char set.<br><br><br><br>Hope this clears up some of the confusion. Feel free to contact me if anyone would like<br>more info.<br><br>Regards,

1	No argument.	<br>I get different transfer rates out of my IDE when I change my ISA bus speed.<br><br><br>IDE is just a variant of the old IBM- MFM AT controller.  (at least that's<br>how it looks from a software point of view).  It was never meant to be<br>an all-encompassing protocal/standard to be implimented across different<br>platforms.<br><br>Is there any argument that <br>IDE can (or can't) transfer data from the IDE drive at least as fast as the<br>drive is able to provide the data?  Are SCSI versions of IDE drives able<br>to deliver higher sustained transfer rates to their SCSI interface (because<br>of a higher RPM platter, different arrangement of heads, etc?)?<br><br><br>If data is going from one drive to another, and if SCSI has the ability to<br>perform that transfer without the data having to go through the CPU or main<br>memory, then yes, that is the optimal way to do it.  As far as I know, IDE<br>can't do that.  But when the CPU wants data from both drives (data to be stored<br>in main memory) are you saying that SCSI can grab data from both drives <br>at the same time *and* store/transfer that data to main memory also at the<br>same time?  Working off 1 IRQ and 1 DMA channel on an ISA (or whatever) bus?<br><br><br>A friend of mine just got a Maxtor 245 meg IDE drive for $320.  (that's 245<br>million bytes, or 234 mega-bytes).  With the basic $20 interface, he gets<br>close to 1 meg/sec transfer on his 286-20.  Does your figure include a few<br>hundred $$$ for SCSI drivers?<br><br><br>So you're saying that SCSI would have been the default interface type,<br>considering that the vast majority of PC's don't have cd-rom drives or<br>tape backups or etc?  That most PC's only have (or had) 1 hard drive and<br>run DOS?  That SCSI hard drives cost a lot more than MFM or RLL drives<br>at the time?  (and how common were SCSI drives under 80 megs 4 to 10 years<br>ago?)  There's a lot more than the lack of a common interface card that<br>prevented SCSI from becoming the connection medium of choice.<br><br><br>But on that point, is it faster?  This is what all this is about.  Do you<br>get more performance for the money.  For all the people that will only have<br>a single hard drive in their system (regardless of the OS) will the SCSI<br>choice really give them more performance than IDE?<br><br><br>True, but expandibility can also start on the bus, which means the option<br>is there for cd-rom drives or tape backups that run off their own cards.<br><br><br>No argument.  This is always held up to the first time SCSI buyer as the<br>best reason.  But how many SCSI devices will the first time SCSI buyer<br>eventually acquire?  Again does it make sense to go SCSI for a single<br>hard drive system?<br><br><br>With all the postings on the SCSI I or II specs, are you really sure that<br>PC and Apple SCSI hard drives are compatible?  And even if they are, <br>is the data accessible from either machine (ie are there no formatting/<br>partitioning or file table differences?)  Is it really plug'n'play?<br><br><br>So the C: drive on the connor becomes a logical D: drive to DOS.  Is this<br>really a problem?  <br><br><br>After having two IDE drives in my system for temporary file transfers,<br>I have never seen any differences when switching between drives, nor<br>have I ever seen any differences when transfering files between drives or<br>to/from the same drive.<br><br><br>That is nice (as long as the power supply can keep up).  I do believe that<br>there is the possibility for up to 4 IDE drives on a PC.<br>

1	This program tests various operations and reports the results<br>in pixels/second.	In going with the modern trend, the Orchid P9000 card only supports 16 colors<br>in 640x480 mode without a driver.  Of course, this breaks any DOS program<br>which uses SVGA modes (like most of my CD-ROMs).  The Compudyne Whiplash VGA,<br>Orchid Fahrenheit 1280, and Orchid F. VLB all share this limitation.  Those<br>are all S3 cards, which means it is an S3 problem for them (the P9000 uses<br>a Weitek VGA chip which also doesn't support them).  The Hercules Graphite<br>card does seem to have these modes, but I didn't run the same test cases as<br>I did on the other boards during the brief time I had it.  It was able to<br>print the splash screen for the Grolier's Encyclopedia, though, which the S3<br>cards just printed as hash, which is why I suspect the SVGA modes are supported.<br><br>The supported resolutions really annoy me.  You can do 1280x1024 at 75Hz if<br>you tell the driver you have an NEC 5FG (they only have about six monitors<br>listed plus 'Generic', and if you choose Generic you can't get any high<br>refreshes at ALL).  But at 1024x768 you are limited to 70Hz.  Seems to me<br>that the hardware should be able to support the bandwidth (if it can do 75Hz<br>at 1280 it sure should be able to do it at 1024!).  Higher vertical resolution<br>was the main reason I bought the card over the Orchid F. VLB I currently have,<br>and it will do 1024x768x70 Hz as well.<br><br>The higher graphics modes all crash HP Dashboard.  I just got off the phone<br>with Orchid, and with the 1.1 drivers (I don't know what I have) he was unable<br>to recreate the problem.  On the plus side, their tech rep was as helpful as<br>he could be and booted up the program on his computer to verify he didn't have<br>the problem.  He didn't know why they limited the refresh to 70 Hz either.<br><br>The board is faster that the OFVLB for most things according to the Hercules<br>Speedy program. This program tests various operations and reports the results<br>in pixels/second.  I don't have the numbers for the Graphite card, but they<br>were close to half of the OFVLB (ie, slower) but that was running in a 20MHz<br>386, ISA, so the numbers aren't really comparable.  The following numbers<br>were all obtained using a 486, 33 MHz, AIR motherboard (UMC chipset), with<br>8 MB memory.  I give ranges because the program reports the numbers as it<br>computes them, and these tend to jump around a bit.<br><br><br>K means thousand (not 1024), M means million, pixels per second<br><br>             Orchid Fahrenheit VLB                  Orchid P9000<br>Chip                 S3 805                          Weitek 9000<br>DIB to Screen        182K - 190K                     228K - 240K<br>Memory to Screen     5.9M - 6.2M                     8.4M - 8.9M<br>Screen to Screen     14M - 14.8M                     29M - 30.8M<br>Vector, solid        2.4M                            2.8M - 2.9M<br>Vector, styled       55K - 58K                       449K - 473K<br>Polygon, shaded      1.8M - 2.1M                     1.6M - 1.9M<br>Polygon, hatched     6.9M - 7.9M                     1.3M - 1.7M<br>Ternary Rops         1.9M - 2.4M                     477K - 520K<br>Font                 130K - 160K                     46K - 55K / 1.2M<br><br>The DIB to Screen test takes a device independent bitmap of a face and transfers<br>it to the screen.  I have no idea what is being done internally as far as<br>conversions go.  The memory to screen takes the same face and copies it to<br>the screen, my guess is after it has been rasterized into a bitmap that can<br>just be copied to the video display.  The screen to screen test copies that<br>face from place to place on the screen.  Awesome!  Interestingly, the solid<br>vectors and shaded polygons show no improvement, and hatched polygons (ie,<br>filled with cross-hatching) and Ternary Rops (whatever they are.  Graphics<br>operations like XORs maybe????) are a dead loss on the 9000.  I give two<br>numbers for the 9000 fonts, because I think they are caching.<br>When the fonts are first drawn on the screen they are done fairly slowly --<br>1/3 the speed of the OFVLB.  Then the speed increases dramatically.  Sounds<br>like programming to a benchmark to me....<br><br>I make no claims that these numbers mean anything at all.  Its just what<br>I saw when I ran them on my computer.  I normally don't write disclaimers,<br>but this time maybe I'd better.  My testing is totally unconnected with my<br>work (I program under UNIX on Decstations) is done completely without the<br>knowledge, blessing, or equipment of my company.

1	Fact is SCSI-2 <br>controler chips allow near SCSI-2 speeds through a SCSI-1 device<br>{As shown in the Mac Quadra} which skews some of the data of SCSI-1 vs<br>IDE or ESDI test.	The article in PC Mag 4/27/93:29 was talking about SCSI-1 {SCSI-2 uses<br>TEN (10) devices in it native mode, outside its native mode it behaves a<br>lot like SCSI-1 (7 devices, slower through put}<br><br>From your own figures SCSI-1 is indeed twice ESDI as the article pointed out<br>as for "20% faster then IDE" that seems to be 8-bit SCSI-1 using a SCSI-2 <br>contoler chip {The Mac Quadra uses a SCSI-2 controler chip for its SCSI-1<br>and gets 6MB/s through put for asynchronous {8-bit} SCSI-1, far in excess of a<br>normal SYNCHRONOUS SCSI-1 output} 120% of 8.3 is 9.96 which is near<br>the burst of a SCSI-1 machine with a SCSI-2 controller chip.<br><br>The PC world seems to have SCSI-1 and SCSI-2 mixed up.  Fact is SCSI-2 <br>controler chips allow near SCSI-2 speeds through a SCSI-1 device<br>{As shown in the Mac Quadra} which skews some of the data of SCSI-1 vs<br>IDE or ESDI test. I agree that the article COULD have stated that the "20%<br>faster then IDE" came off a SCSI-1 device with a SCSI-2 chip.  Maybe it<br>was there and the EDITOR killed it because the article was dealing with<br>SCSI-1 NOT SCSI-2 and he did not understand the effect of a SCSI-1 device<br>with a SCSI-2 controller chip.<br>SCSI-1 chips are limited to 5/MB max.  SCSI-1 devices with SCSI-2 chips<br>{becoming common} produce up to 10Mb/s in 8-bit mode and 20MB/s in 16-bit<br>mode {the fast version, SCSI-1 ports cannot use wide SCSI}.  Of cource<br>the prime piece of wierdness is that SCSI-1 devices HAVE SCSI-2 chips<br>{or more accurately the machine does}. This allows the best of BOTH<br>worlds: high SCSI-2 speeds and cheeper SCSI-1 costs {FULL SCSI-2 hardware<br>(port, electronic controller, etc) is VERY expensive.  It ALSO creates<br>a logistic NIGHTMARE as to how fast SCSI-1 goes.

1	ISA has no bus arbitration, so if two busmastering cards in 1 ISA system<br>try to do DMA xfers on the same DMA channel the system will lock or <br>crash.	Seriously, busmastering adapter have their own DMA ability, they don't use<br>the motherboards on-board DMA(which is *MUCH* slower).<br><br>ISA has no bus arbitration, so if two busmastering cards in 1 ISA system<br>try to do DMA xfers on the same DMA channel the system will lock or <br>crash.(I forget)<br><br>Their are 8 DMA channels in an ISA system. 0-7. 0-3 are 8-bit & 4-7 are<br>16-bit.<br><br>The system uses DMA 0, a SoundBlaster uses DMA 1.<br><br>I could buy a busmastering XGA-2 video card & a busmastering SCSI HA.<br><br>In order for them to work properly, I would have to find out what DMA<br>channel the XGA-2 card uses and then simply configure the SCSI HA to<br>use a different DMA channel for its DMA xfers.<br><br>I don't know if multiple DMA xfers can go on at the same time on ISA.<br>I'm not sure if they can on EISA systems either.<br><br>I do know that on EISA/MCA systems, you can allow BM cards to use the<br>same DMA channel.

I mess with this older<br>stuff alot.   Kinda fun.  8)<br><br>     First problem I can forsee is that the ST-251 will not be<br>compadible with that WD card unless it has the right bios rom.  <br><br>    Check the numbers on it.  It should be the only non-smt chip on<br>the board.  Slightly below center, and left.    The bios should read <br>either :     62-000042-015 or<br>             62-000094-0x2<br><br>     If the last 3 digits are 013, you got problems.<br><br><br>    Looks right.   W5 and W7 are factory jumped (with a trace) between<br>pins 1 and 2 to select the primary controller address.<br><br><br>    Looks right.<br>  <br>[art deleted]<br><br><br>      I notice you left out the S1 jumper table settings.   Those are<br>what control what drive the controller thinks it has.   If you have<br>the 62-000042-015 rom, set it like this:<br><br>              5 + +    open<br>              6 + +    open             <br>              7 + +    open        <br>              8 + +    open<br>              4 + +    closed<br>              3 + +    closed<br>              2 + +    open<br>              1 + +    open<br><br>    Note:   those are how WD runs the numbers on the jumper block.<br>Top to bottom.   +'s represent the jumper pins.    Pins 3,4, and 8<br>select the first drive setting (drive 0) and pins 1,2 and 7 select the<br>second drive (drive 1).   <br><br>      If you have the 62-000094 rom, it's a auto-config, and I'll have<br>to look up how to do it... I don't have the big book right here.<br><br><br>      See above.  You might have problems if the S1 jumpers are not<br>right.   Also,  at the risk of being insulting, make sure the cables<br>are on right and good. 8).    On the jumper on the 251, try moving it<br>to the opposite side of the drive.    It's one or the other.   <br>     The narrow data cable goes to J2.   I've thrown it on J3 a few<br>times and banged my head for a day.....<br><br><br>     Dunno.    IBM roms had to be later than 10/27/82.   A quick way<br>to check is to boot dos and run debug.   Enter:<br>       <br>        -d f000:fff5 fffc    (the - is the debug prompt)<br>   <br>    This will return the rom date, if it's of any use.<br><br><br>      If I oversimplified any of the above, I appologize.     It's<br>just hard to know what caliber of person I'm talking to. 8).<br><br>                     -->   Bob Valentine  <--  <br>                  --> ravalent@mailbox.syr.edu <--  <br><br><br><br>From chpp@unitrix.utr.ac.za Mon Apr  5 06:33:46 1993<br>Received: from unitrix.utr.ac.za by ponder (5.61/1.36)<br> id AA16194; Mon, 5 Apr 93 06:32:59 -0500<br>Received: by unitrix.utr.ac.za (Smail3.1.28.1 #1)<br> id m0nfpMA-0001X7C; Mon, 5 Apr 93 13:28 GMT<br>Message-Id: <m0nfpMA-0001X7C@unitrix.utr.ac.za><br>From: chpp@unitrix.utr.ac.za (Prof P. To solve this problem, one of the ROM BIOS chips <br>          must be disabled.  To disable the BIOS on your Western Digital  <br>          XT controller card, you must remove the jumper at position W-3 <br>          or add a jumper at position R-23 (depending on which model of XT <br>          controller you are using).<br>          <br>                                       -2-<br><br><br>          <br>          NOTE 2:  TANDY 1000 SYSTEMS - The WD1002A-WX1, WD1004A-WX1, <br>          WDXT-GEN2 and the WD1004-27X can be modified to operate in <br>          Tandy 1000 series computers, models SX, TX and the original or <br>          "A" version.  These computers utilize an interrupt of 2 (IRQ2) <br>          instead of IRQ5, the IBM standard.  To modify the WD1002A-WX1 or <br>          the WD1002-27X to operate in these systems, you must cut the <br>          etch between pin 1 and pin 2 at jumper position W-7.  Then <br>          solder pin 2 and pin 3 at the position (W-7).  To complete the <br>          modification, a jumper must be added to position 7 of switch S-1 <br>          (2 rows of 8 pins).  PLEASE NOTE THAT ANY PHYSICAL MODIFICATION <br>          TO YOUR WESTERN DIGITAL HARD DISK CONTROLLER VOIDS THE WARRANTY <br>          ON YOUR BOARD.  To modify the WD1004A-WX1, WDXT-GEN2 or the <br>          WD1004-27X for your Tandy 1000 system, a zero ohm resister must <br>          be soldered to jumper position W-27. 0	TrueType font files are at least 1/3 larger than their Type-1<br> equivalents.	    1. TrueType font files are at least 1/3 larger than their Type-1<br> equivalents.  If you are using a disk compressor, though, be aware<br> that TrueType fonts will compress, whereas Type-1 fonts will not<br> (they are encrypted).<br><br>    2. With a 300 dpi laser printer, the "artificial bold" version of a<br> TrueType font is indistingishable from the original, forcing me to<br> install the actual bold (and bold-italic, etc.) variants if I want<br> to use them.  Type-1 PostScript fonts under ATM generate a visually<br> distinct bold variant from the base font.  I realize that the<br> artificial bold font that ATM generates is aesthetically inferior<br> to the hand-generated bold TrueType variant, but it is sufficient<br> for my needs, and not having to install bold variants saves me 50%<br> on disk space (uncompressed).<br>

0	With the hardware scroll enabled, applications write garbage outside the<br>  window.	I have (had) several problems with the card.<br>- Applications crashed when moving the mouse (VGA mode). Fixed by removing<br>  the line STACKS=0,0 from config.sys. The default value STACKS=9,256<br>  does just fine.<br>- Random white pixels all over the screen when moving the mouse, in a<br>  256 color graphical mode (the hardware cursor modes). Fixed by turning<br>  off the hardware scroll ("24xmode scrolloff") before entering the graphics<br>  mode. This also fixed the problem of the windowed DOS boxes under windows.<br>  With the hardware scroll enabled, applications write garbage outside the<br>  window.<br>- The screensavers do not remove the cursor in 256 color modes (hardware<br>  cursor). I don't know if this is a windows problem or a driver problem.<br>  Clearly there should be two types of cursor removing functions: a remove-<br>  for-draw, which can be ignored for hardware cursors and a remove-<br>  unconditionally. 0	by reducing<br> the variety of instructions & addressing modes), the hardware can be<br> designed to run faster.	(Taken from one of my lecture notes)<br><br> " ...  The alternative approach (to CISC),<br> which has been adopted by many in<br> recent years, has come to be known as "RISC": the Reduced<br> Instruction Set Computer.  This can be characterised simply as<br> "Simpler is Faster"; by simplifying the design (e.g.  by reducing<br> the variety of instructions & addressing modes), the hardware can be<br> designed to run faster.  Even at the cost of needing more<br> instructions, the same task can be done more quickly by the simpler,<br> faster design.<br><br> A typical RISC processor will:<br><br>    o provide a large number of registers (e.g.  32);<br><br>    o perform all data operations on registers;<br><br>    o provide few addressing modes (e.g. immediate or 'register + offset');<br><br>    o only allow load & store operations to access memory;<br><br>    o only use a few instruction formats;<br><br>    o only support a few data types (e.g. integer, unsigned, floating).
0	Like<br>magic, all of your problems will go away.	In a nutshell the problem is that I lose the first 0.625 inches of<br>information from my left margin, be it white space or TrueType font<br>output, and margins are not preserved on subsequent pages past the<br>first.<br><br>WordPerfect had a workaround consisting of using the "Default" location<br>for the printers instead of "Tractor" or "Manual".  They have also filed<br>this as a bug and are continuing to investigate it.<br><br>MS Write, of course, has no problem with these printer drivers, proving that<br>Microsoft knows something the rest of us don't!  Are you surprised?  I'm<br>not.<br><br>Publish It!/WIN is still investigating this problem, and while I was consider-<br>my options (rejecting the one about buying an $800 DTP package, for *surely*<br>they wouldn't have this problem, right?) I stumbled onto a global workaround.<br><br>WORKAROUND<br>----------<br>Go into the Windows 3.1 control panel, select printers, select your Citizen<br>printer driver, select SETUP, and select a custom size of 850 x 1132.  Like<br>magic, all of your problems will go away.<br><br>Ask me no questions and I'll tell you no lies!<br><br>-- Mike<br><br><br>------------------------------------------------------------------------<br>Disclaimer: My opinions do not necessarily reflect those of my employer.<br>========================================================================

1	For this reason, I use the<br>"factory defaults" for position/size on the ATI card<br>and adjust each mode individually (only the first time)<br>at the monitor.	I thought this was a neat feature until I noticed that<br>when an image is re-sized, the scanning frequency is<br>necessarily changed.  This causes digital multiscan<br>monitors like my MAG MX17F to get confused as to which<br>mode to use if the frequency gets too far from the<br>standard selections.  For this reason, I use the<br>"factory defaults" for position/size on the ATI card<br>and adjust each mode individually (only the first time)<br>at the monitor.  The MAG (and many other multiscan<br>monitors) has (have) the ability to recall these settings<br>the next time each mode is "detected".<br>-- <br><br>=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=

1	Hope that this message is not over your head, but the answer to your <br>question was not simple.	don't believe everything you are told.  I can tell you that mixing them <br>between 'banks' ok, and I can't see why mixing in one bank is not unless <br>they are of different speeds ( e.g. mixing of 60ns and 100ns SIMMs in one <br>bank ).  The two only differ in the type of chips it uses.  Assuming that <br>the SIMMS are 1Mx9 ( 9 bit wide ), here is the two equivalent <br>configuration.  The 3-chip SIMM uses two 4-bit wide 4Mbit (1M of 4-bit <br>nibbles ) and one 1-bit wide 1Mbit chip ( for a total of 9-bit wide 1Mbyte <br>).  The 9-bit SIMM uses nine 1-bit wide 1Mbit chips.  These are equivalent <br>because of the way that it is 'pinned' on the SIMM board.  At the SIMM <br>interface, they both act as 9-bit wide 1MByte SIMMS ( 2*4+1=9*1 ).  [sorry <br>if too techie for ya].<br><br><br>MG>>  Similarly, one cannot plug in two 1MB SIMMs and one 4MB SIMMs to <br>gi<br>MG>> the system a total of 6 MEG. Why is that so ?? If my system supports<br>MG>> of 8 MEG (it has 8 SIMM slots), can I plug in 4 4MB SIMMs to give my<br>MG>> 16MB ??<br><br>That sounds correct.  the problem is that if your computer takes 9-bit <br>wide SIMMs, you can not mix different sizes in one bank.  Why you ask?  <br>Simple,  if you understand why there is banks.  Assuming you have a 32-bit <br>CPU ( 386DX or 486 ), the data bus (e.g. the mechanism to retrieve data <br>from memory ) is 32-bits wide, so the computer expects to see 32 bits when <br>it asks for data.  To get that bandwidth ( 32-bit wide ), the motherboard <br>links 4 1Mx9 ( one bit is not data, but parity, so I will ignore that in <br>this simple explaination ) to get 32bits [ (9-1)*4=32 bits ].  That means <br>that a SIMM in a bank stores only 1/4 of the 32 bit wide data.  If you <br>have a 16-bit bus, two 1Mx9 SIMMs are linked together to get 16-bit wide <br>data, which is the reason why 286 banks are 2 SIMMs wide, and 32-bit banks <br>are 4 SIMMs wide.  If your computer required 1Mx36 ( e.g. 32-bit wide data <br>with 4 parity bits, used in some PS/2s and ASTs ), you could upgrade by <br>one SIMM at a time.<br><br>Hope that this message is not over your head, but the answer to your <br>question was not simple.  I could of just said, 'because I said so.'<br><br>-rdd    <br><br>---<br> . WinQwk 2.0b#0 . Unregistered Evaluation Copy<br> * KMail 2.95d W-NET HQ, hal9k.ann-arbor.mi.us, +1 313 663 4173 or 3959<br>

1	The menu system 'should' use<br>DOS calls to get the time, which would cause the DOS 'kernel' to do<br>the BIOS call, and the wrap indicator would hence be processed<br>properly.	There are two 'problems':<br>(1) the BIOS TOD routine which updates the BIOS clock uses only 1 bit<br>    for day increment, so a second wrapping of the clock past midnight<br>    will get lost if no one calls the BIOS to read the clock in the<br>    meantime, and<br>(2) the BIOS resets the day wrap indicator on the first 'get date'<br>    call from ANYBODY (after the wrap indicator has been set). So<br>    unless the first BIOS 'get date' call after midnight is done by<br>    the DOS 'kernel' (which is the only part of DOS which knows how to<br>    increment the date, the day wrap indication is normally lost.<br>My guess is that Kevin's 'menu' system uses BIOS calls to read the<br>clock (in order to display the time), and is hence the entity which<br>causes the day wrap indication to get lost. Even if the 'menu' system<br>'notices' the day 'wrap' (which I think is indicated by a non-zero<br>value in AL), there really isn't any particularly good way to tell DOS<br>about it, so that DOS can update the day. The menu system 'should' use<br>DOS calls to get the time, which would cause the DOS 'kernel' to do<br>the BIOS call, and the wrap indicator would hence be processed<br>properly.  Possibly, though, the 'menu' system can't easily use DOS<br>calls for time, because DOS is not reentrant, and perhaps time<br>incrementing ofters occur while the 'menu' system is 'inside' some<br>other DOS call.

1	A SCSI device works on many different machines (I have a mac<br> and a PC at home and moving hard drives between them is VERY nice<br> with SCSI -- hook them up and away they go)<br> 3.	: >IDE:  Integrated Device Electronics <br>: > currently the most common standard, and is mainly used for medium sized <br>: > drives. Can have more than one hard drive. Asynchronous Transfer: ~5MB/s max.<br><br>: Why don't you start with the spec-sheet of the ISA bus first?<br>: You can quote SCSI specs till you're blue in the face, but if they<br>: exceed the ISA bus capability, then what's the point?<br><br>Who said ISA was necessary?  EISA or VLB are the only interfaces worth<br>investing thousands of dollars (e.g. a new pc's worth of money ) in .<br><br>: Who says IDE is limited to 5 megs/sec?  What about VLB-IDE?  Does anyone<br>: know how they perform?<br><br>You didn't read to carefully.  VLB-IDE uses the same connection mechanism<br>as standard IDE.  If transfer rate is limited by IDE, whether it's<br>interfaced to ISA, EISA or VLB matters not.<br><br>: >So at its LOWEST setting SCSI-2 interface in Asynchronous SCSI-1 mode AVERAGES <br>: >the through put MAXIMUM of IDE in asynchronous mode.  In full SCSI-2 mode<br>: >it blows poor IDE out the window, down the street, and into the garbage can.<br>: As implimented on what system?  <br><br>On mine, for one thing.  SCSI blows IDE out of the water, hands down.  If<br>IDE has better throughput, why isn't it used on workstations and file<br>servers?  <br><br>: >The problem becomes can the drive mechanisim keep up with those through put<br>: >rates and THAT is where the bottleneck and cost of SCSI-2 comes from.  NOT<br>: >the interface itself but more and more from drive mechanisims to use the<br>: >SCSI-2 through put.  <br><br>: Given the original question (SCSI used only as a single hard drive<br>: controller),  is it then necessary to get a SCSI drive that will do<br>: at least 5, maybe 10 megs/sec for the SCSI choice to make any sence?<br>: What does a 200-400 meg 5 megs/sec SCSI drive cost?<br><br>No, that's the nice thing -- on a multitasking OS, SCSI can use both drives<br>at once. I paid $369 for it.  I<br>haven't seen IDE drives cheaper.<br><br>: The original CGA cart back in '84 was $300.  I think the original EGA card<br>: (or PGA?) was $800.  SCSI has stood relatively alone in not coming down<br>: in price, mainly because we're talking about PC's and not Sun's or Sparc<br>: or SGI or (name your favorite unix workstation).  That is, after millions<br>: of PC buying decisions over the years, SCSI has had plenty of time to<br>: come down in price.<br><br>No, actually, we're talking about SCSI being expensive simply because<br>nobody did a common interface for the PC.  If they had a common (read:<br>easily implemented) method of adding scsi to a PC (like as in a Sun or<br>Mac), then you'd find SCSI the connection medium of choice.<br><br>: I won't argue that the SCSI standard makes for a good, well implimented<br>: data highway, but I still want to know why it intrinsically better<br>: (than IDE, on an ISA bus) when it comes to multi-tasking OS's when<br>: managing data from a single SCSI hard drive.<br><br>On a single drive, SCSI is more expensive.  But, you bought your PC for<br>expandibility, so, you'd want to add more drives or whatever.  The<br>following are why I find SCSI intrinsically better than IDE:<br><br>A (partial?) list:<br> 1.  You can add many different types of devices and access them <br> concurrently.<br> 2.  A SCSI device works on many different machines (I have a mac<br> and a PC at home and moving hard drives between them is VERY nice<br> with SCSI -- hook them up and away they go)<br> 3.  SCSI devices work together better than IDE devices.  For<br> instance, recently, I added an older connor 100 meg IDE to a maxtor<br> 212 meg IDE.  The connor *MUST* be setup as the slave.  It will<br> work no other way.  On SCSI, you set the address, check the<br> termination, plug it in, and away it goes.<br> 4.  I have a problem with IDE's mutual exclusion - I notice that<br> the time it takes to switch from accessing drive c: to drive d: is<br> quite long as compared to the time it takes to switch from drive c:<br> to d: on a SCSI system.  Under a multitasking OS, this is very<br> noticable, as many things can be going on at once.<br><br>One neat thing that I've noticed lately (a fringe benefit) has been the<br>ability to add older (almost dead) drives as storage on a SCSI system with<br>little problem -- we've got a bunch of almost dead 20 meg drives that I've<br>added to my PC.  I've now got the interface full, but, it does allow me to<br>have 4 20 meg drives, 1 240 meg drive, 1 tape drive, and 1 105 meg drive<br>all on the same card.  <br><br>Simply put, SCSI is handier than IDE.  No mysterious jumpers to figure out.<br><br>Greg.

0	They<br>still use the same acronym, but only to be familiar.	RISC used to mean "Reduced Instruction Set Computer", true.  They<br>still use the same acronym, but only to be familiar.  What RISC really<br>means is a recently-designed CPU.  :-)<br><br>In general, most RISC CPUs are like this:<br><br>    A large number (32 or more) of general-purpose registers<br>    A fixed instruction size, usually 32 bits<br>    An instruction may make only one memory reference<br>    Memory references must be aligned<br>    There are delayed branches (branch after the next instruction) or<br>a target prediction bit (probably will/won't branch)<br>    Instructions may complete out of order relative to the instruction<br>stream

PKTMUX is required if you are using<br>different TCP/IP-Packages concurrently.<br><br><br>There is the first problem: You didn't specify hostnames, just IP-Addresses.<br>Your file should look like this:<br><br>137.112.5.2        <Name of your Nameserver><br>137.112.199.50     <Name of your Router><br><br><br>Here you should only specify your hostname, without the domain part.<br><br><br>Instead, use router=<Name of your Router>, as specified in your QVTHOST.RC<br>I know the release notes for 3.31 say that IP addresses should work also,<br>but  apparently they don't.<br><br><br>Here, too, you should use the hostname of the nameserver instead of the<br>IP address.<br><br>It worked fine for me that way, although I could not specify more than<br>one nameserver. 0	Unforutnately, once the page run-on has<br>occured you are hosed.	<br><br><br><br>I contacted Lotus about this problem today.  It has been reported previously<br>and there is a fix.  Apparantly the problem only occurs when TAB characters<br>are used immediately preceding the equation frame.  The work around when<br>equations are expected to touch the right margin is to delete at least one<br>preceding TAB and use SPACE to align the frame (or use set frame where<br>placed w/no text wrap around).  Unforutnately, once the page run-on has<br>occured you are hosed. So the moral of the story is use only SPACE characters<br>to align equation frames.<br><br>Hope this helps the rest of you who have already contacted me with this <br>problem.

0	The data area is organized as clusters of a fixed size; storage is<br>doled out in "chunks" of one cluster each.	<br>You'll probably want to delete any damaged executables and reload them<br>fresh.  Data files should be examined and repaired.<br><br><br>You're not stupid.  Smartdrive caches things, and Windows also runs a<br>swap file which may contain data also.  All of this is pretty risky<br>stuff in a PC environment.<br><br><br>A reasonably accurate prediction...<br><br><br>Here's a brief description of how DOS stores files:<br><br>There are three pieces to a file.  The directory entry, the FAT chain,<br>and the data area.  You can think of these as a sheet of lined<br>notebook paper, a sheet of graph paper, and a stack of 3X5 cards.<br><br>The directory entry (notebook paper) holds the file name, actual size,<br>and first cluster number.  It also holds some other information that's not<br>important right now.<br><br>The File Allocation Table (FAT) chain (graph paper) tells where to find<br>the actual data.  Each square of graph paper holds a number.  If the<br>number is zero, the cluster associated with this box is available.  If it<br>holds a "magic" number, it is either the last piece of a file or a bad<br>(unuseable) spot on the disk.  Any other number tells which cluster<br>contains the next section of the file.<br><br>The data area (3X5 cards) is where the actual information is stored.<br>The data area is organized as clusters of a fixed size; storage is<br>doled out in "chunks" of one cluster each.  (In your case, one cluster<br>is 2048 bytes.)  As a cluster is filled, another is allocated.<br><br>To read a file, you first look at the directory entry to get the<br>starting cluster number.  Now you read the data from that cluster.<br>Next, look at the FAT entry for the cluster you just read.  This will<br>tell you the cluster number for the next chunk of the file.<br>Naturally, these numbers are usually sequential, but they can jump<br>around and even go backwards.  Continue reading, one cluster at a<br>time, as you walk this chain through the FAT, until you hit the marker<br>which says it's the last cluster in the file.<br><br>CHKDSK is the DOS utility that checks the sanity and coherence of<br>the directories and the FAT and can bludgeon most flaws into<br>submission.  It doesn't have any intelligence, so you have to<br>double-check anything it "fixes".<br><br>Now let's do a bit of a post-mortem:<br><br><br>All of these files have sizes (according to the FAT) which don't match<br>the size reported in their directory entries.  CHKDSK /F will alter<br>the directory entries to match the FAT size.  (In other words,   the<br>directory entry for CVPIC.EXE may say the file is 64,877 bytes long.<br>But CHKDSK found a FAT chain of 43 clusters attached to it.  (My numbers,<br>obviously, are made up.))<br><br><br>Disk space was found which is allocated in the FAT, but is not attached<br>to any directory entry.  CHKDSK /F gives you the option of converting these<br>"lost chains" to files.  You can then examine the files (FILE0000.CHK<br>through FILE0223.CHK) and rename or discard them.  Or, if you tell<br>CHKDSK _not_ to convert them to files, then those clusters will simply<br>be marked "available" in the FAT.<br><br><br><br>...<br><br><br>These files are both referencing the _same_ data cluster.  Obviously,<br>one of them (at least) must be wrong.  It's interesting to note that<br>C:\386SPART.PAR is your Windows swap file...<br><br>To fix this, you should copy each cross-linked file to a new name.<br>This will "fix" the cross-link by giving the files unique data spaces.<br>Now delete the cross-linked files.  Examine the copies and try to<br>assemble them properly.  Good luck.  (Hint:  "Missing pieces" are<br>likely to be found in those "lost chains" at the top...)<br><br><br>Your disk is pretty close to full.  This may be the actual cause of<br>the problem; perhaps Windows needed to expand its swapfile by an<br>amount which exceeded available disk space...<br><br>In any case, the short summary is that something trashed your FAT.<br>There are utilities that can mirror your FAT and help repair damage<br>after something like this, but you have to run them _before_ the<br>problem occurs.  Sorry.<br><br><br>-- <br>----------------------------------------------------<br>Gordon S. Hlavenka           cgordon@vpnet.chi.il.us<br>Vote straight ticket Procrastination party Dec. 3rd!<br>--

The IDE interface is<br>fundamentally nothing more than an extension of the ISA bus, and if<br>you hook it to VL-bus it'll work as fast as the slower of the two,<br>meaning ISA speed.<br><br><br>On the other hand, I wouldn't expect it to be *slower*...

1	There are THREE key differences in SCSI; the controller chip, the port, and<br>the software.	SCSI is not complex. It is just the way the industry uses and talks about it.<br>There are THREE key differences in SCSI; the controller chip, the port, and<br>the software.  THAT IS IT.<br>Let us look as SCSi in from THIS stand point.<br><br>SCSI-1: asynchronous and synchronous modes {SOFTWARE SCSI DRIVER ONLY}<br> asynchronous is slower then synchronous mode {only 0-3MB/s vs. 0-5MB/s}<br> synchronous speeds can be reached by most SCSi-1 divices with a<br> rewrite of the software driver {As is the case for the Mac Quadra.}<br><br>SCSI-2 {8-bit}: THIS is the main source for the confusion. This differs from<br> SCSI-1 ONLY in the controler chip in most machines.  In the Mac and some PCs<br> this is called 'fast SCSI-1' because it uses SCSI-1 ports and software drivers<br> AND can produce SCSI-2 SPEEDS through SCSI-1 INSPITE of this even in the <br> slower asynchronous mode.  Average speed in asynchronous SCSi-1 mode <br> 4-6MB/s with 8MB/s{See in both Quadras and higher end PCs} Synchronous<br> mode just allows a higher burst rate {10/MB/s}<br><br>SCSI-2 {16-bit}: TWO versions-Wide/Fast.  Wide SCSI-2 requires TWO things<br> over 8-bit SCSI-2:  a SCSI-2 software driver and a wide SCSI port on the<br> machine and the external device.<br> Fast SCSI-2 also requires TWO things over 8-bit SCSI-2: SCSI-2 driver<br> software and that the RECIEVING devise support 16-bit fast SCSI-2.<br> Speed of both is the same: 8-12MB/s with 20MB/s burst.<br><br>SCSI-2 {32-bit}:  Also know as Wide AND Fast SCSI.  Over 8-bit SCSI-2 this<br> requires: SCSI-2 driver software, wide SCSI-2 port, and that the RECIEVING<br> devices ALSO have a 32-bit mode SCSI-2 chip.  As expected this is VERY<br> expencive.  Speed: 15-20MB/s with 40MB/s bursts<br>

 Display 22 on 7-segment LEDs<br>            HLT<br>            END<br><br>                              C++ using BIOSCOM()<br><br>#include <stdio.h><br>#include <stdlib.h><br>#include <bios.h><br><br>#define INIT          0<br>#define SEND          1<br>#define RECEIVE       2<br>#define STATUS        3<br><br>#define COM2          1<br><br>void<br>main()<br>{<br>   char abyte;<br><br>   abyte = 0xa3;<br>   bioscom( INIT, abyte, COM2 );<br>   printf( "Initialized COMM PORT 2\n" );<br><br>   while( !( bioscom( STATUS, 0, COM2 ) & 0x4000 ) )<br>     ;<br>   abyte = ' ';<br>   bioscom( SEND, abyte, COM2 );<br>   printf( "Sent start character\n" );<br>}<br><br><br>           C++ using INT86()<br><br>#include <stdio.h><br>#include <stdlib.h><br>#include <dos.h><br>#include <bios.h><br><br>main()<br>{<br>   union REGS registers;<br>  <br>   registers.h.ah = 0x00;<br>   registers.h.al = 0xa7; NAME:   comp.os.ms-windows.nt.setup<br>STATUS:  Unmoderated.<br>PURPOSE: Discussions about setting up and installing Windows NT, and about<br>  system and peripheral compatability issues for Windows NT.<br><br>NAME:  comp.os.ms-windows.nt.misc<br>STATUS:  Unmoderated.<br>PURPOSE: Miscellaneous non-programming discussions about using Windows NT,<br>  including issues such as security, networking features, console<br>  mode and Windows 3.1 (Win16) compatability.<br><br>RATIONALE:<br> Microsoft NT is the newest member of the Microsoft Windows family<br> of operating systems (or operating environments for those who wish<br> to argue about the meaning of an "OS").  The family ranges from<br> Modular Windows through Windows 3.1 and Windows for Workgroups to<br> Windows NT at the high end.  To date, Microsoft has shipped over<br> 50,000 beta copies and pre-release SDKs of Windows NT -- the<br> actual release is slated for May/June '93.<br><br> While Windows NT has an entirely new design internally, it shares<br> an application programming interface with the other members of the<br> Windows family; its Win32 API includes the Win16 API used in Win-<br> dows 3.1, and the Win32s API subset (Win32 less threads, networking<br> and security) can be used to create 32-bit applications for<br> Windows 3.1.<br><br> The user interface is also practically identical to that of Windows<br> 3.1, with the addition of logins and a few other features.  It uses<br> Program Manager, File Manager and other applets, and generally pre-<br> sents an identical appearance to the user.  Many of the announced<br> Windows NT applications are ports of existing Windows 3.1 apps, and<br> NT also runs existing 3.1 applications.<br><br> Thus, it appears logical that Windows NT should share the following<br> groups with the other members of the Windows family:<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.apps<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.programmer.tools<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.programmer.misc<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.programmer.win32<br> <br> The following groups are also clearly applicable to Windows NT as<br> well as Windows 3.1:<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.announce<br>  comp.os.ms-windows.advocacy<br> <br> In conclusion, the only clear argument for the separation of the<br> Windows 3.1 and Windows NT hierarchies is different internal<br> structures of Windows 3.1 and Windows NT.  And yet operating<br> systems such as OS/2, Macintosh OS, Xenix and Coherent all have<br> undergone major rewrites without having been split into separate<br> newsgroup hierarchies.<br><br> Further, Windows 3.1 is due for a major rewrite itself in 1994 --<br> when the fully 32-bit, protected-mode and with-DOS-built-in next-<br> generation Windows, "Chicago", debuts next year, surely it should<br> remain in the same hierarchy.  And what, then, would be the jus-<br> tification for separating Windows NT from other Windows versions?<br><br><br>DISCUSSION PERIOD:<br> The discussion period will run from 27 April, 1992 to 18 May, 1993.  <br><br>VOTING:<br> The CFV (Call for Votes) will be issued around 19 May, 1993, based on<br> the feedback received during the discussion period.  No votes will<br> be accepted prior to the CFV.

0	The<br>       cache will save up so much that when it grabs control of the machine<br>       it takes too long to write to disk and you loose characters.	Try the following:<br>   1) Turn off disk write cache for the disk you are downloading to.  The<br>       cache will save up so much that when it grabs control of the machine<br>       it takes too long to write to disk and you loose characters.<br><br>   2) Use a different UART for your serial line.  The old UART's (8250 or 16450)<br>       can only buffer one character internally.  The new UART's (16550) can<br>       buffer 16, which should be plenty for most situations.  You can run<br>       \windows\msd.exe to find out what UART is on the machine.

1	For serial RS-232 <br>25-pin ports, you'll need a female DB-25 connector with pins 1 to 7;<br>2 to 3; 4 to 5 to 8; 6 to 11 to 20 to 22; 15 to 17 to 23; and 18 to 25<br>connected.	From a recent BYTE magazine i got the following:<br><br>[Question and part of the answer deleted]<br><br>  If you are handy with a soldering iron, the loopback plugs are easy to<br>make.  On a serial RS-232 nine-pin port, use a female DB-9 connector and<br>connect pins 1 to 7 to 8; 2 to 3; and 4 to 6 to 9.  For serial RS-232 <br>25-pin ports, you'll need a female DB-25 connector with pins 1 to 7;<br>2 to 3; 4 to 5 to 8; 6 to 11 to 20 to 22; 15 to 17 to 23; and 18 to 25<br>connected.  To test a Centronics 25-pin parallel port, you'll need to<br>connect pins 1 to 13; 2 to 15; 10 to 16; 11 to 17; and 12 to 14 in a male<br>DB-25 connector.<br><br>     -Stan Wszola<br>---<br><br>I haven't tried it. Use at own risk.

1	if you're really paranoid, or you just have an 8th wire to spend,<br>you might add:<br> FG(1)   -  FG(1)<br><br>the pin numbers above are (obviously) for 25-pin connectors.	as someone else already mentioned, don't "carry thru" the other 23 pins.<br><br>plan A, minimal null modem:<br> SG(7)   -  SG(7)<br> TD(2)   -  RD(3)<br> RD(3)   -  TD(2)<br>if you're transfering files betwen 2 PCs, this ought to work (unless<br>you have a persnickity comm program).<br><br>plan B, null modem with modem control:<br> SG(7)   -  SG(7)<br> TD(2)   -  RD(3)<br> RD(3)   -  TD(2)<br> DCD(8)  -  DTR(20)<br> DTR(20) -  DCD(8)<br>some comm software will wait for DCD before transferring anything.<br>such software will raise DTR to enable the modem to accept a call.<br>with this cable, each side's DTR will drive the other's DCD.<br><br>plan C, null modem with hardware flow control:<br> SG(7)   -  SG(7)<br> TD(2)   -  RD(3)<br> RD(3)   -  TD(2)<br> RTS(4)  -  CTS(5)<br> CTS(5)  -  RTS(4)<br>for high-speed transfers, you want the flow control.<br><br>plan D, deluxe null modem (combine B and C):<br> SG(7)   -  SG(7)<br> TD(2)   -  RD(3)<br> RD(3)   -  TD(2)<br> RTS(4)  -  CTS(5)<br> CTS(5)  -  RTS(4)<br> DCD(8)  -  DTR(20)<br> DTR(20) -  DCD(8)<br>this one is ideal.  it leaves out DSR and RI (rarely used anymore).<br>if you're really paranoid, or you just have an 8th wire to spend,<br>you might add:<br> FG(1)   -  FG(1)<br><br>the pin numbers above are (obviously) for 25-pin connectors.<br>i don't have a 9-pin pinout handy.<br>
0	(If I had<br> a number to call at HP or Adobe, they'ld have heard from me.)	Q: Which version of Adobe PS will we clone?<br> Aside from the level 1 and level 2 issues, Adobe has in the past released<br> new code that incorporates modifications/upgrades/fixes just as all other<br> software vendors do. The level 2 stuff may seem sound now, but I assure<br> you,changes will become more frequent as their customer list begins to<br> dwindle in the face of competition. This allows them to shift people to<br> maintenance, as well as design efforts for level 3.<br><br>Q: Do we duplicate the bugs, or do we make it work correctly?<br> From the LaserWriter to the LaserJet 4 there have been bugs. (If I had<br> a number to call at HP or Adobe, they'ld have heard from me.) Deciding<br> which approach to take depends on which printer you want to emulate.<br><br>Q: Do we follow the Red Book, or do we follow someone's implementation?<br> Without a doubt, there are differences between the Red Book and Adobe's<br> PS. With level 2 many issues have been refined but the Red Book does<br> leave big, big holes in the implementation specific stuff. It would be<br> nice it the Red Bood at least pined things down enough so that two<br> different implementations of Adobe's PS don't do the exact opposite given<br> an identical set of conditions.<br><br>Q: PSCRIPT.DRV?<br> Having done a lot of PS clone testing myself, the unfortunate side of<br> testing is the limited number of sources for test files. The primary<br> source we use is Genoa. And having characterizes their 1992 PS ATS files,<br> (1300+ of them) over half are taken from PSCRIPT.DRV. It may not ideal,<br> but the ATS files are what the printer vendors use. I'm sure that Adobe<br> uses them too, but Adobe's output is by definition correct, even if its<br> wrong.<br><br>Yes, there are some very poor clones. We've seen them here at Rastek (a sub<br>of Genicom which has its own clone called GeniScript). Some are poor for lack<br>of design, some are poor because they followed the Red Book, and some are poor<br>because the vendors don't know what PS is.

0	Your net worth can be computed from this <br> information.	I've been using Managing Your Money for several years, and I have<br>several friends who use Quicken, though I've not used it myself.  My<br>overall impression is that Quicken is a financial accounts manager<br>while Managing Your Money will help you more completely manage your<br>finances.<br><br>Here are some features that I believe Managing Your Money and Quicken share:<br><br> The ability to keep records for each of your financial accounts:<br> checking, savings, charge, cash, or brokerage.  You can reconcile<br> your account statements with the records the program keeps.<br><br> The ability to make a budget and track your spending against that<br> budget.<br><br> A Checkfree module which will allow you to use the Checkfree bill<br> paying service to pay your bills via your modem.<br> <br> The ability to print checks on your printer.<br><br> The ability to keep loan records and set up automatic loan payments.<br><br> The ability to import stock quotations to keep your brokerage accounts<br> up to date.  I know Managing Your Money can do this automatically<br> via modem.  Quicken probably can as well, but I'm not sure about it.<br><br> The ability to export tax information to popular tax preparation<br> programs.<br><br><br>Here are some features that I believe Managing Your Money has that<br>Quicken does not:<br><br> A tax prediction module.  This looks at your accounts and budget to<br> predict your tax liability for the coming year.  It's usefull to fine<br> tune your withholding so Uncle Sam doesn't get his due too early.<br><br> An insurance and vital records module.  This is a place to keep<br> records of your insurance policies as well as other vital records.<br> It can also compute your life expectancy.<br><br> A financial analysis module.  This computes compound interest, <br> effects of inflation, loan payments term or interest, yield to<br> maturity for bonds, savings account yields, days between dates, and<br> loan amortization schedules.  It also has functions to help you <br> decide whether to refinance your mortgage, whether you can afford<br> a particular home, whether a particular rental property is a good<br> investment, whether you should buy lease or rent, and whether<br> a particular investment's cash flow is adequate for your situation.<br> This module can also help you plan for retirement and for helping<br> your kids with their tuition.  <br><br> A net worth module.  This is a place to record all your assets<br> and liabilities.  Your net worth can be computed from this <br> information.  Any assets or liabilities recorded in other modules<br> are automatically included here.<br><br> A "desk" module.  This includes a small word processor, a card file<br> you can use to store names, addresses, phone numbers and other vital<br> information about friends and associates, a perpetual calander, a<br> rudimentary calculator, a to-do list, a reminder list, an appointment<br> list, and a place to record your phone calls (for those who need to<br> track such things).  Your appointments, reminders and to-do list<br> can be made to display automatically when you start the program.<br><br>There are probably some things listed above that Quicken has, but I'm almost<br>sure that Quicken doesn't do everything I've listed.  If I'm wrong, I'm sure<br>hordes of Quicken devotees will flame me to a crisp.<br><br>One thing that Quicken has that Managing Your Money does not yet have is a <br>Windows version.  MECA software is rumored to be working on a Windows version<br>of Managing Your Money for release late this year.

0	Next, define a transaction group which uses these memorized transactions<br>and specify the frequency that it should be used (i.e.	Hi<br>     I have never used MYM so I can not help you with the comparison of the<br>two products.  I am, however,  a devoted Quicken user, and I can tell you<br>how to set up the weekly, monthly, quarterly, yearly transactions.  First<br>use the Memorize feature (CTRL-M) to record the recurring transactions. <br>Next, define a transaction group which uses these memorized transactions<br>and specify the frequency that it should be used (i.e. monthly).  Quicken<br>will/can not automatically make the transactions for you, but now all you<br>do is recall the group and all of the individual transactions will be<br>entered at once.  Additionally if you are using the Bill Minder, it will<br>remind you when each transaction group is due.  For example, you could have<br>three transaction groups.  One for payments at the beginning of the month,<br>middle of the month, and one for quarterly payments.  I hope this helps.

1	Our<br>camera images came out very good in my opinion.	The quality difference is very noticable and is almost worth the wait (for<br>the PS processing) - I'm rather impatient.<br><br>We were using it for B&W camera images (RS-170).  The gray scale image was<br>accepted by MS Word and handed to the Winjet PS printer driver which converts<br>the image into postscript and then hands it off to the Winjets postscript<br>processor.  The postscript is rendered into RAM (lots of it) and when it<br>is done it shoots it directly to the printer.  The PS processor can also<br>accept PS files created from other sources including DOS applications, but<br>Windows has to be running at the time of printing.  The PS processor is<br>responsible for the halftoning and I'd say it does a pretty good job.  Our<br>camera images came out very good in my opinion.  (not as good as Laser Master's<br>demo though).<br><br>I don't know how many other similar products are out there but I would be<br>surprised if there are several.
1	Obviously, since time<br>    is used as a position, you cannot get rid of this ridiculus waste <br>    of time.	<br><br>    The joystick reads in anolog values through a digital port. How?<br>    You send a command to the port to read it, then you time how long<br>    it takes for the joystick port to set a certain bit.  This time<br>    is proportional to the joystick position.  Obviously, since time<br>    is used as a position, you cannot get rid of this ridiculus waste <br>    of time.  If you wrote your own routine instead of the BIOS, it<br>    would speed it up some, but the time would still be there.<br><br>--

1	I also reviewed a new Nanao, the F550iW, which has just<br>been released.	<br><br><br>One of the monitors I reviewed for the June issue of Windows Magazine was<br>the Mitsubishi.  I also reviewed a new Nanao, the F550iW, which has just<br>been released.  Last year for the May '92 issue of Windows, I reviewed<br>several monitors, including the Nanao T560i.  There's no question that the<br>Nanao monitors are the best available this year, just as they were last<br>year.  The difference between my ranking of the best in Windows and the<br>ranking in Windows Sources is due mainly to a difference in the testing<br>criteria and the scoring.  I used different tests than they did, and I<br>scored differently.  There's nothing wrong with the Mitsubishi, and it<br>scored very highly in my tests, but it was a few points shy of perfect.<br><br>Incidentally, one of the things everyone should do when they're reading<br>reviews of any product, whether it's monitors or mice, is to read the<br>criteria and methodology carefully.  Unless you know how the product<br>testing was done, and on what the scores are based, you can't possibly<br>know what they really mean.  Just seeing that I rank a monitor differently<br>from Windows Sources is meaningless without knowing how we did the<br>ranking.  Likewise, it's impossible to tell whether a monitor will meet<br>your needs unless you know how we did the testing.  After all, some of<br>what we do may not apply to you.  Likewise, some of what we do may apply<br>more closely in one review than in another.  You can't always tell<br>anything from reading the 300 or so words of commentary we write if you<br>don't also understand the scoring.

0	You have to be holding the Ctrl key when you<br>RELEASE the mouse button if you want to force a copy operation.	I tried to respond by email, but all attempts bounced.<br><br>The condition of the Ctrl key BEFORE you press the mouse button makes no<br>difference whatsoever.  You have to be holding the Ctrl key when you<br>RELEASE the mouse button if you want to force a copy operation.<br><br>Here's a simple experiment.  Select a file and begin to drag it (no Ctrl<br>key).  Notice that the file's icon disappears from the listing window.  Now<br>watch what happens to that icon as you press and release the Ctrl key<br>(keeping the mouse button pressed all the while).  In addition, the icon<br>that you are dragging will show a "+" while you are holding the Ctrl key,<br>indicating that the file is being copied rather than moved.<br>

1	The cost of SCSI interface is a self fulliling<br>prophisy: few people buy SCSI because it is so expencive for the PC, which<br>in turn convices makes that mass producing SCSI {which would reduce its<br>cost} is unwarented, and so SCSI is expencive.	IBM<br>HD Interfaces {limited to hard drives by design or lack of development}:<br>[...]<br>IDE:  Integrated Device Electronics <br> currently the most common standard, and is mainly used for medium sized <br> drives. Can have more than one hard drive. Asynchronous Transfer: ~5MB/s max.<br><br>So at its LOWEST setting SCSI-2 interface in Asynchronous SCSI-1 mode AVERAGES <br>the through put MAXIMUM of IDE in asynchronous mode.  In full SCSI-2 mode<br>it blows poor IDE out the window, down the street, and into the garbage can.<br>The problem becomes can the drive mechanisim keep up with those through put<br>rates and THAT is where the bottleneck and cost of SCSI-2 comes from.  NOT<br>the interface itself but more and more from drive mechanisims to use the<br>SCSI-2 through put.  The cost of SCSI interface is a self fulliling<br>prophisy: few people buy SCSI because it is so expencive for the PC, which<br>in turn convices makes that mass producing SCSI {which would reduce its<br>cost} is unwarented, and so SCSI is expencive. {That is the effect of the<br>Rule of Scale: the more items sold the less EACH item has to bare the brunt<br>the cost of manufacture and so the less each item has to cost}

Buy from my competition<br>and you'll be sorry if you need service." <br><br>Experienced mail order buyers know that there are some mail order<br>companies that give excellent service, including overnight replacement parts, <br>on-site calls, etc. There are probably some local dealers that can give you<br>good service, too. 1	Though it is WELL documented that the Quadra has a SCSI-2 chip<br>an Apple salesperson said "it uses a fast SCSI-1 chip" {Not at a 6MB/s,<br>10MB/s burst it does not.	SCSI-1 {with a SCSI-1 controler chip} range is indeed 0-5MB/s<br>and that is ALL you have right about SCSI<br>SCSI-1 {With a SCSI-2 controller chip}: 4-6MB/s with 10MB/s burst {8-bit}<br> Note the INCREASE in SPEED, the Mac Quadra uses this version of SCSI-1<br> so it DOES exist. Some PC use this set up too.<br>SCSI-2 {8-bit/SCSI-1 mode}:          4-6MB/s with 10MB/s burst<br>SCSI-2 {16-bit/wide or fast mode}:  8-12MB/s with 20MB/s burst<br>SCSI-2 {32-bit/wide AND fast}:     15-20MB/s with 40MB/s burst<br> <br>By your OWN data the "Although SCSI is twice as fast as ESDI" is correct<br>With a SCSI-2 controller chip SCSI-1 can reach 10MB/s which is indeed<br>"20% faster than IDE" {120% of 8.3 is 9.96}. ALL these SCSI facts have been<br>posted to this newsgroup in my Mac & IBM info sheet {available by FTP on <br>sumex-aim.stanford.edu (36.44.0.6) in the info-mac/report as <br>mac-ibm-compare[version #].txt (It should be 173 but 161 may still be there)}<br><br>Part of this problem is both Mac and IBM PC are inconsiant about what SCSI<br>is which.  Though it is WELL documented that the Quadra has a SCSI-2 chip<br>an Apple salesperson said "it uses a fast SCSI-1 chip" {Not at a 6MB/s,<br>10MB/s burst it does not. SCSI-1 is 5MB/s maximum synchronous and Quadra<br>uses ANsynchronous SCSI which is SLOWER}  It seems that Mac and IBM see<br>SCSI-1 interface and think 'SCSI-1' when it maybe a SCSI-1 interface driven<br>in the machine by a SCSi-2 controller chip in 8-bit mode {Which is MUCH<br>FASTER then true SCSI-1 can go}.

On 14" Sony tubes, you'll find one wire.<br><br>Their level of annoyance is purely subjective. 1	NO.	<br>If you ran dos, you don't need a 16550, because dos runs only ONE task at a<br>time and the whole cpu-power could be used for the transfer. But if you are<br>running a multitasking OS such as OS/2, Unix, etc. ( windows ? :-) ), the<br>cpu cannot work the whole time with one task. <br><br>The result are lost characters or broken transmissions because of timeouts.<br><br><br>NO. The only diffrence is that internal modems have the UART on board,<br>whereas external modems are connected to the computer over a serial port<br>which has the UART on board.<br><br><br>I use a Zyxel1496B with a 16550UART under COHERENT 4.0. I'm very satisfied<br>with it but I think that nearly everyone is satisfied with his own modem.<br><br>Michael
