1	Here are some relevent<br>quotes from the manual:<br><br>  "Data is transferred from the disk to the read buffer at a rate of<br>   3.75 MB/s maximum, 1.87MB/s minimum."	What is the deal with the IDE transfer rates?  Is anybody getting throughput<br>anywhere close to the platter->controller rate?  I haven't seen anything<br>even close to the 5MB/sec limit of the IDE interface.  These drives are 1:1<br>(non-interleaved), aren't they?<br><br>Here are the rates I get:<br><br>1) sequential read (MSDOS C program that uses bios calls to read 64-sector<br>                    blocks sequentially from outside (first) track inward)<br><br>                     8mhz bus    10mhz bus<br>                     --------    ---------<br>   MAXTOR LXT340:    860KB/sec   976KB/sec<br>   QUANTUM LPS240:   862KB/sec   887KB/sec<br><br>2) disk buffer read (same C program, but re-reads the same block repeatedly<br>                     so in effect is reading the RAM buffer on the drive)<br><br>                     8mhz bus    10mhz bus<br>                     --------    ---------<br>   MAXTOR LXT340:   1046KB/sec  1212KB/sec<br>   QUANTUM LPS240:  1015KB/sec  1276KB/sec<br><br>3) CORETEST V2.7 transfer rate (seems to agree with (2) above)<br><br>                     8mhz bus    10mhz bus<br>                     --------    ---------<br>   MAXTOR LXT340:   1051KB/sec  1224KB/sec<br>   QUANTUM LPS240:  1026KB/sec  1298KB/sec<br><br>I managed to get hold of the QUANTUM LPS240AT product manual and it goes<br>into excrutiating detail describing how the bits get from the platter,<br>through the controller, and out the IDE interface.  Nowhere do I see<br>anything like "after the bits are whipped of the platter at high speed<br>they sit around in a buffer to thaw before they are sent to the host"<br>(even though I SWEAR that's whats happening ;->).  Here are some relevent<br>quotes from the manual:<br><br>  "Data is transferred from the disk to the read buffer at a rate of<br>   3.75 MB/s maximum, 1.87MB/s minimum."  (My calculations show 3121KB/sec<br>   maximum and 1578KB/sec minimum...  disk spins at 4306 RPM with 87<br>   sectors per track on the outside and 44 on the inside)<br><br>  "Single burst errors of up to 24 bits within one sector can be corrected<br>   'on-the-fly', in real time as they occur, allowing a high degree of<br>   data integrity with no impact to the drive's performance."  (I take<br>   this to mean error correction isn't the bottleneck)<br><br>  "For page-mode operations, the data-transfer rate to and from the buffer<br>   RAM is up to 10.0 MB/s. This high transfer rate allows the AT Interface<br>   IC to communicate over the AT bus at a data-transfer rate of 5.0 MB/s,<br>   while the DCS simultaneously controls disk-to-RAM transfers"<br>                 ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^<br>So the thing can even do it's cache pre-fetch WHILE it's sending the<br>requested sector (it has 3 64KB read buffers for pre-fetching, I guess<br>you could call that a cache :-|).<br><br>So when I do a sequential read on the outer tracks, WHY am I getting a<br>measly 862KB/sec when I should be getting around 3MB/sec???<br><br>Any of you hard-disk engineers out there know?<br>

Anyway, I<br>just wanted to see if anyone else had any trouble and what they did about<br>it.  Any feedback will be appreciated.<br><br>The system configuration is:<br><br>Gateway 486 DX2/66 Local Bus<br>16 Megs Ram<br>SCSI HD & CD-ROM<br>Ultrastor 34F Local Bus SCSI controller<br>ATI Ultra Pro Local Bus with 2MB VRAM<br>DOS 6.0<br>Windows 3.1<br>Mach 32 drivers version 1.5 (build 59)<br><br>Thanks in advance.
1	* Windows configuration:<br><br>     To get the Adaptec 1542 to work with Windows, make sure that the<br>"[388Enh]" section of the SYSTEM.INI file contains the entry:<br><br> VirtualHDIRQ=Off<br><br>I believe that the Windows install program automatically adds this entry<br>to SYSTEM.INI, but I'm not sure.	     Here's a document that I wrote some time back.  It's slightly<br>out-of-date, now that DOS 6 has been released, but much of it is still<br>useful.<br><br>     -- Darryl Okahata<br> Internet: darrylo@sr.hp.com<br><br>DISCLAIMER: this message is the author's personal opinion and does not<br>constitute the support, opinion or policy of Hewlett-Packard or of the<br>little green men that have been following him all day.<br><br>===============================================================================<br>$Id: adaptec.txt 1.8 1993/01/25 00:55:08 darrylo Rel darrylo $<br>          Hints and Tips for the Adaptec 1540/1542 SCSI adapter<br><br><br>     This document contains hints and tips for getting the Adaptec<br>1540/1542 SCSI adapter to work with various hardware and software<br>packages.  They are based upon my experiences with an Adaptec 1542A<br>controller, and will, hopefully, help others.  However, note that I<br>cannot guarantee that the following will really help you (it works for<br>me), and the information in this document could possibly cause you to<br>lose some or all of your files on your hard disk.<br><br>     IMPORTANT!  BACK UP THE ENTIRE CONTENTS OF YOUR HARD DISK BEFORE<br>TRYING ANYTHING BASED UPON INFORMATION IN THIS DOCUMENT.<br><br>     Copyright 1993, by Darryl Okahata.  This document may be freely<br>copied for personal use only, and may not be reprinted in a for-profit<br>publication without the consent of the author.  Please note that I have<br>no connection with Adaptec other than as a customer.<br><br>Topics covered in this document:<br><br> * Windows 3.1 enhanced mode<br> * Floppy-controller-based tape backup devices<br> * Sound cards<br> * Miscellaneous info<br><br>     Please note that parts of this document contain technical, and<br>sometimes terse, descriptions of problems.<br><br>For reference:<br><br> Adaptec technical support: (800) 959-7274<br> Adaptec BBS (2400/9600): (408) 945-7727<br><br>Please send comments, corrections, etc. via email to me:<br><br> CompuServe: 75206,3074<br> Internet: darrylo@sr.hp.com<br><br><br>***** Windows 3.1 enhanced mode:<br><br>     The Windows 3.1 install program should automatically configure DOS<br>and Windows for use with the Adaptec 1542.  However, just in case<br>something went wrong, I'm going to describe some of the changes needed<br>to get Windows 3.1 working with the 1542.  Also, you may have noticed<br>that installing Windows 3.1 makes your PC run much slower, even when<br>you're not running Windows; methods of speeding it up are discussed in<br>the section called, "Windows 3.1 runs slowly".<br><br><br>* MSDOS configuration:<br><br>     The Windows install program adds the SmartDrive disk cache to your<br>CONFIG.SYS and AUTOEXEC.BAT files.  If you follow the instructions,<br>you'll notice that you'll need to use double-buffering with SmartDrive<br>(this is the default setup).  You'll also notice that your system runs<br>much, much slower -- in both Windows *AND* MSDOS.  See the section<br>called, "Windows 3.1 runs slowly", for some ways of speeding your system<br>up.<br><br><br>* Windows configuration:<br><br>     To get the Adaptec 1542 to work with Windows, make sure that the<br>"[388Enh]" section of the SYSTEM.INI file contains the entry:<br><br> VirtualHDIRQ=Off<br><br>I believe that the Windows install program automatically adds this entry<br>to SYSTEM.INI, but I'm not sure.  If this doesn't work for you, you<br>might want to try adding some more lines:<br><br> VirtualHDIRQ=Off<br> SystemROMBreakPoint=false<br> EMMExclude=A000-CFFF<br><br>(You probably don't need the above lines, though.)  The<br>"SystemROMBreakPoint" entry is used to enable support for memory<br>managers like QEMM/386MAX (only needed if you use such programs).<br><br><br>* Windows 3.1 runs slowly:<br><br>     Once you do get Windows 3.1 running with the 1542, chances are that<br>your system is running much slower than before.  If it's not, it's<br>probably because:<br><br>     1. You happen to be using ASPI4DOS.SYS version 3.1 in your<br> CONFIG.SYS file.  Congratulations -- this appears to be a<br> winning solution.<br><br>     2. You are very lucky.  Whether your luck will hold out remains to<br> be seen ....<br><br>If your system is running much slower than before, this is almost<br>definitely caused by Smartdrive with double-buffering.  According to the<br>Windows documentation, and the Microsoft technical note #Q81808<br>("SMARTDrive Double Buffering Required with ASPI4DOS.SYS"), you must use<br>Smartdrive with double-buffering enabled.  While this works, it really<br>slows down your PC; I once estimated that this slowed my PC down by a<br>factor of 5 (FIVE).  As I consider this unacceptable, I looked for other<br>solutions.<br><br>     Unfortunately, you cannot just disable double-buffering.  If you<br>do, Windows 3.1 in enhanced mode will not work, and you might even<br>destroy the contents of your hard disk by trying to run Windows 3.1.<br>What you can do is one of the following:<br><br>     1. Use other drivers that provide double-buffering.  It is my<br> opinion that the unbelievable slowness in Smartdrive is caused<br> either by horribly inefficient double-buffering, or by a bug in<br> Smartdrive.<br><br>     2. Use a driver that provides "VDS" services ("VDS" stands for<br> "Virtual DMA Services").  This is a standard, which is supported<br> by Windows 3.1, that allows bus-mastering disk controllers (like<br> the 1542) to work with Windows.<br><br>     After trashing my hard disk countless times, I found the following<br>solutions, none of which require using Smartdrive (note, however, that I<br>am now getting occasional parity errors, which are probably *NOT* caused<br>by these solutions, but might be -- see below).  While the following<br>does not require Smartdrive, using some kind of disk cache utility is<br>strongly recommended, as this makes Windows run much, much faster:<br><br>1. If you do not have the ASPI4DOS.SYS driver, or you do not need ASPI<br>   functions (for controlling a CDROM, tape drive, more than two<br>   physical hard disks, etc.), you can add the SCSIHA.SYS driver to your<br>   CONFIG.SYS file, e.g.:<br><br> DRIVER=c:\SCSIHA.SYS /V386<br><br>   (Windows needs the "/V386" option.)  This driver MUST be loaded into<br>   LOW memory (it cannot be loaded into high memory), and it occupies<br>   about 16-20K.  As of November 1992, the SCSIHA.SYS driver could be<br>   obtained from the Adaptec BBS at (408)-945-7727 (hopefully, it's<br>   still there).<br><br>2. If you need ASPI functions and have the ASPI4DOS.SYS driver, version<br>   3.0 or 3.0a, you can use both the ASPI4DOS.SYS and SCSIHA.SYS drivers<br>   in your CONFIG.SYS file, e.g.:<br><br> DRIVER=c:\ASPI4DOS.SYS<br> DRIVER=c:\SCSIHA.SYS /V386<br><br>   Amazingly enough, the SCSIHA.SYS driver can also be loaded high<br>   (assuming you have DOS 5.0); I would have thought that this would<br>   crash my system, but it doesn't.  I asked Adaptec's technical support<br>   about this, and they said that loading SCSIHA.SYS high should be fine<br>   as long as ASPI4DOS.SYS is loaded LOW.<br><br>   On my system, NOT using SCSIHA.SYS with ASPI4DOS 3.0a would<br>   occasionally cause Windows 3.1 to crash upon restarting or exiting<br>   Windows, with the additional result of a corrupted disk (some of my<br>   C:\WINDOWS\*.GRP files would be corrupted).  For me, these crashes<br>   usually occurred while making a different program from PROGMAN.EXE<br>   the default Windows shell, and vice-versa.  This is the reason<br>   SCSIHA.SYS may be necessary.<br><br>   I have absolutely no idea if SCSIHA.SYS is necessary with versions of<br>   ASPI4DOS earlier than 3.0.<br><br>   Note that many people can use ASPI4DOS 3.0 or 3.0a without<br>   SCSIHA.SYS; they do not seem to have any problems at all.  I consider<br>   these people lucky.  Others, like me, have had all sorts of problems.<br><br>3. In my opinion, the best, but not necessarily the easiest, solution is<br>   to upgrade to ASPI4DOS 3.1.  The SCSIHA.SYS driver is no longer<br>   needed.  Unfortunately, while you could get previous ASPI4DOS<br>   upgrades from the Adaptec BBS, the ASPI4DOS 3.1 driver is not<br>   available from the Adaptec BBS.  As far as I know, there are only<br>   three ways to get a copy:<br><br>      * You can buy the new (as of November 1992) Adaptec EZ SCSI driver<br> kit, which supposedly includes ASPI4DOS 3.1 as well as other<br> drivers, such as CDROM drivers.  I believe the list price is<br> around $75.<br><br>      * If you already have a copy of an older version of ASPI4DOS, you<br> can supposedly contact Adaptec to upgrade it to EZ SCSI for<br> around $30.<br><br>      * A copy of ASPI4DOS 3.1 is included in Central Point PC Tools 8.0<br> for MSDOS.  Note that the documentation and driver are stored in<br> different directories.  Note further that only ASPI4DOS is<br> included; the CDROM drivers and drivers to support more than two<br> hard disks are not included.  This is where I obtained my copy<br> of ASPI4DOS 3.1.<br><br>Note, however, that I am now getting occasional parity errors with<br>Windows.  In all probability, defective hardware in my PC is causing<br>this, as I upgraded my motherboard just after I found the above<br>solutions.  However, because these parity errors occur only during disk<br>accesses, there is a very small, but definite, possibility that the<br>parity errors are driver-related (for example, changing the bus on/off<br>timing for certain disk transfers might cause this).  I've run various<br>memory tests for hours at a time, and these tests have found no<br>problems.  This problem is probably caused by memory with marginal<br>timing requirements, which cause parity errors during disk transfers<br>(this is why the memory tests didn't find any problems -- the problems<br>show up only under disk I/O).  However, I'm mentioning this just in case<br>it isn't a hardware problem.<br><br><br>***** Floppy-controller-based tape backup devices:<br><br>     There are two possible problems with using the Adaptec 1542 with a<br>floppy-controller-based tape backup device, such as the Colorado Memory<br>Systems Jumbo 250:<br><br>1. Tape backups/restores can take a very long time.  The tape drive<br>   constantly starts, stops, starts, stops, etc.<br><br>2. Tape operations may be erratic, or encounter too many tape errors.<br>   (This problem might be caused by defective hardware on my 1542.<br>   However, I've heard of other people having similar problems, and so<br>   I'm mentioning this just in case it is not a hardware problem on my<br>   1542.)<br><br><br>* Tape backups/restores take a long time:<br><br>     If you have a floppy-controller-based tape backup device, you may<br>have to adjust the Adaptec 1540/1542 "bus on/off timing" for best<br>results when using the tape drive.  Normally, while doing a tape backup<br>or restore, the tape drive motor should be continuously running, with<br>only an occasional pause.  However, the default bus timing on the<br>Adaptec 1540/1542 may cause the tape drive motor to start and stop,<br>start and stop, every few seconds.  This causes needless wear to the<br>tape and tape drive (however, note that a dirty tape head or a defective<br>tape drive can also cause this -- make sure your tape heads are clean).<br>This also causes the tape backup or restore to take much, much longer<br>than necessary.<br><br>     The problem here is that these tape backups use the floppy DMA to<br>transfer data in memory to/from the tape drive, and the Adaptec uses DMA<br>to transfer data in memory to/from the hard disk.  The floppy DMA needs<br>to feed data to the tape drive at a certain rate; if the tape drive is<br>not fed data quickly enough by the floppy DMA, the tape drive stops,<br>rewinds a bit, and restarts (once enough data is eventually fed to it).<br>The default bus timing on the Adaptec (which is really DMA timing) is<br>"too large".  For example, when a backup is done, data has to be<br>transferred from a hard disk to memory, and then from memory to the<br>tape.  Because the default timing on the Adaptec "hogs" the memory too<br>much (too much time is spent transferring data from a hard disk to<br>memory), not enough time is spent transferring data from memory to the<br>tape drive.  As a result, the tape drive constantly starts and stops,<br>because data is not fed to it quickly enough.<br><br>     The solution is to change the Adaptec's bus on/off timing.  The<br>default factory setting is 11 microseconds on, and 5 microseconds off.<br>The "bus on" timing needs to be lowered to 2-4 microseconds.  This can<br>be done in one of two ways:<br><br>* If you have ASPI4DOS, you can use the "/n" option.  For example, I use<br>  a "bus on" timing of 4 microseconds, which means that I use the<br>  following line in my CONFIG.SYS file:<br><br> DEVICE=c:\aspi4dos.sys /n4<br><br>  Note that there is NO space between the "/n" and the "4".<br><br>* If you don't have ASPI4DOS, your only recourse is to try to find a<br>  program called "SETSCSI.EXE", which is very difficult to find.  The<br>  reason is that Adaptec, for reasons of their own, does not seem to<br>  want this widely distributed.  I once asked someone who worked for<br>  Adaptec, and they asked me to not upload it anywhere.  If you have<br>  anonymous ftp access to the Internet, you could try using archie to<br>  hunt down a copy; I believe that there are a couple of sites that have<br>  it.  If you do find a copy, you run it like so:<br><br> setscsi -n:4<br><br>  This adjusts the "bus on" timing to 4 microseconds.  Running<br>  SETSCSI.EXE without any arguments resets the bus timing back to the<br>  factory defaults.<br><br>  Note that it seems that you cannot use SETSCSI.EXE if you use<br>  ASPI4DOS; SETSCSI.EXE crashed my system if ASPI4DOS was loaded.  I<br>  could use SETSCSI.EXE with SCSIHA.SYS, however.<br><br>Do not lower the "bus on" timing below 2 microseconds, or increase it<br>above 11 microseconds.  If you lower it too low, the hard disk<br>throughput will suddenly drop; your system will feel slower.  For me, 4<br>microseconds works fine.  This value may work fine for you, or you may<br>have to adjust it downwards a little.<br><br>     Once you've lowered the "bus on" timing, tape backups and restores<br>should run faster.<br><br>     Also, do not experiment with the bus on/off times (with the other<br>options that I have intentionally not described), unless you know what<br>you are doing.  Bad combinations can cause parity errors and worse, by<br>starving memory refresh.<br><br>     A program called BUSTIFIX.EXE exists on the Adaptec BBS.  Unless<br>this has been upgraded since I last checked (which has been a while),<br>this is a self-extracting archive containing a batch file and a couple<br>of other files.  This batch file was supposed to allow one to set the<br>bus on/off times for the 1540/1542 and others.  However, when I tried<br>running this program with my 1542A, my system crashed.  At the time, I<br>was running SCSIHA.SYS, and I didn't check to see if there was a<br>conflict with it.  Maybe this old program works only with the 1542B,<br>although the docs say that it works with the 1542A?<br><br><br>* Erratic tape operations or too many tape errors:<br><br>     This "problem" may or may not exist.  Although it existed on my<br>system, a hardware problem just on my particular 1542 could cause it.<br>However, I've heard of other people having similar problems, and so I'm<br>mentioning this just in case it isn't a hardware problem just on my<br>1542.<br><br>     Symptoms of this "problem", which persists even after cleaning the<br>tape head:<br><br>1. Backing up to tape encounters "unusable sector detected" errors,<br>   resulting in an aborted tape backup.<br><br>2. Tape backup works, but the tape compare fails.<br><br>3. The tape drive starts, stops, starts, stops, etc. much too often.<br>   Unlike the above-mentioned problem ("Tape backups/restores take a<br>   long time"), where the tape drive starts and stops every few seconds,<br>   this kind of starting/stopping occurs every few 10-20 seconds or so.<br><br>4. Fastback Plus 3.1 does not find/see any tape backup devices.  Other<br>   programs, like Central Point Backup and the CMS Jumbo software<br>   (assuming that you have a CMS Jumbo 250 tape drive) can find/see the<br>   tape drive, but Fastback Plus 3.1 cannot.<br><br>5. Too many tape read errors.<br><br>     Although I do not know what is causing this problem, I discovered<br>that using a different floppy controller solves it.  A few months ago, I<br>upgraded my motherboard, which contained an integrated floppy<br>controller.  As I already had a floppy controller on the 1542, I<br>initially disabled the motherboard floppy controller.  After a while, I<br>decided to try disabling the 1542 floppy controller and using the one on<br>the motherboard.  When I did this, the tape drive (a CMS Jumbo 250)<br>reliability increased dramatically, and Fastback Plus 3.1 was suddenly<br>able to find and use the tape drive.<br><br>     I don't know if this was caused by a hardware problem on my 1542.<br>On the one hand, the floppy drives worked great when they were attached<br>to the 1542, which seems to say that there was nothing wrong with the<br>1542.  On the other hand, the tape drive didn't work well attached to<br>the 1542 floppy controller, but it did work when attached to a different<br>controller; this could be an indication of a hardware problem on my<br>1542.  I did change floppy drive cables, and so it is conceivable that<br>the problem was in the cables.  I don't know what the cause really is;<br>however, if you're having similar problems, you might want to consider<br>trying a new floppy controller.<br><br><br>***** Sound cards:<br><br>     Many popular sound cards can play or record digitized sound, and<br>this is typically done using DMA.  Like the tape drive DMA, the<br>Adaptec's DMA can conflict with the sound card DMA.  Unlike that of the<br>tape DMA, this "conflict" usually manifests itself as a parity error<br>(your system crashes with a parity error message).  What happens is<br>that, data is being transferred so quickly by the sound card and the<br>Adaptec, memory refresh cannot occur quickly enough, which causes a<br>parity error.  Usually, getting a parity error means that there is a<br>hardware problem with your system; in this case, however, the parity<br>error is not a symptom of bad hardware.<br><br>     I've found that such parity errors typically occur while recording<br>digitized sound, and the chances of such errors increase as you increase<br>the recording fidelity (e.g., higher sampling rate, recording in stereo,<br>recording using 16-bits instead of 8, etc.).<br><br>     Like the tape drive solution, the solution here is to lower the<br>Adaptec's "bus on" timing.  See the section on tape drives for<br>information on how this is done.  Note, however, that this may or may<br>not solve the problem; it may only reduce the probability of a parity<br>error.  The software used to record digitized sound can greatly affect<br>this problem (i.e., some software is inefficient).  Disk caches, the<br>speed of your hard disk, and the amount of disk fragmentation can also<br>affect this.<br><br><br>***** Miscellaneous info:<br><br>     This section contains miscellaneous hints, tips, and rumors.  Much<br>of it is merely information that I've heard or read about, and have not<br>verified.  I believe that the following information is correct, but I'm<br>not sure.  Use it at your own risk.<br><br>* With QEMM 6.00, 6.01, and 6.02, you need to specify the "DB="<br>  parameter (e.g., "DB=2"), unless you are using the ASPI4DOS driver.<br>  If you don't, QEMM will crash/hang at bootup.  Although the QEMM<br>  manual mentions this, the install program does not seem to detect that<br>  a 1542 is present and automatically add this option to the QEMM<br>  command line (at least, this occurred with the QEMM 6.00 install<br>  program -- I haven't tested any other version).  Earlier versions of<br>  QEMM probably need this parameter, but I'm not sure (I've never used a<br>  version earlier than 6.00).<br><br>  If you use ASPI4DOS, you do not need to give QEMM the "DB=" parameter.<br><br>* Some or all versions of the 1542 do not support hard disks over one<br>  gigabyte in size.  To support hard disks with capacities over 1GB, you<br>  need to get a new ROM BIOS from Adaptec.  I'm not sure if this is<br>  still true of the latest 1542Bs being sold by Adaptec.<br><br>* To connect a CDROM drive to the 1542, you need a SCSI CDROM drive and<br>  some drivers.  Note that some CDROM drives have proprietary interfaces<br>  (non-SCSI); these drives cannot be used with the 1542.  You have three<br>  choices for CDROM drivers (I have no idea how well the following<br>  solutions work, or even if they work -- the following is secondhand<br>  information):<br><br>     1. You can buy Adaptec's EZ SCSI driver package, which lists for<br> something like $75.  If you already have older Adaptec drivers,<br> you can supposedly upgrade to EZ SCSI for around $30.  Contact<br> Adaptec for details.  The EZ SCSI package supposedly contains<br> everything that you need.<br><br>     2. You can buy the CorelSCSI! driver package, which is made by the<br> same people that make CorelDRAW!  This package contains CDROM<br> drivers, SCSI tape drivers, WORM drivers, etc.  I do not know<br> the list price, but I've seen this package sold for around<br> $80-$90.  Note that CorelSCSI! does not come with the ASPI4DOS<br> driver, which is needed.  If you do not already have ASPI4DOS,<br> you may be better off getting Adaptec's EZ SCSI instead.<br><br>     3. [This method is obsolete, as the following drivers have been<br> obsoleted by Adaptec's EZ SCSI kit, but I'm mentioning it in<br> case someone already has these drivers.]  You can use the<br> drivers in the Adaptec ASW-1410 kit (ASPI4DOS) and the ASW-410<br> kit (ASPI CDROM drivers).  You will have to get a copy of<br> MSCDEX.EXE (a high-level CDROM driver), if it is not included in<br> the ASW-410 kit, but this is available from several bulletin<br> boards.<br><br>* To use a SCSI tape drive with the 1542, you need software that knows<br>  how to talk to a SCSI tape drive.  Software that I've heard about are<br>  (again, like the above section on CDROM drives, I have no idea how<br>  well the following solutions work, or even if they work -- the<br>  following is secondhand information):<br><br>     1. Central Point PC Tools 8.0 for MSDOS supposedly supports a large<br> number of SCSI tape drives.  It comes with SCSI drivers<br> (ASPI4DOS 3.1) as well as Central Point Backup.<br><br>     2. The CorelSCSI! driver package contains a SCSI tape backup<br> program (see the above section on CDROM drives for more<br> details).  However, note that CorelSCSI! does not come with, but<br> requires, ASPI4DOS.<br><br>* I've seen advertisements that sell the 1542 in three configurations:<br><br>     1. 1542 SCSI controller with hard disk ROM BIOS.<br>     2. 1542 SCSI controller w/BIOS and Adaptec ASPI drivers.<br>     3. 1542 SCSI controller w/BIOS, Adaptec ASPI drivers, and<br> CorelSCSI! drivers/programs.<br><br>  I imagine that Adaptec now sells the 1542 in a fourth configuration:<br><br>     4. 1542 SCSI controller w/BIOS and EZ SCSI drivers (including ASPI<br> drivers).<br><br>* Those people who use Unix might be interested in a version of GNU tar<br>  for MSDOS that talks to a SCSI tape drive via the ASPI4DOS driver (you<br>  need this driver before you can use this program).  I've never used<br>  this version of GNU tar, but I've heard that it works (I don't know<br>  how well, though).  If you have anonymous ftp access to the Internet,<br>  a copy can be found on wsmr-simtel20.army.mil and mirror sites:<br><br> PD1:<MSDOS.DSKUTL><br> ASPIBIN.ZIP  67841 920131 Gnu Tar for SCSI tape drives, Adaptec 154xx<br> ASPIPAT.ZIP  21206 920131 Patches for ASPIBIN relative to Gnu Tar 1.10<br> ASPISRC.ZIP 221370 920131 Src for Gnu Tar for SCSI tape, Adaptec ctrlr<br><br>  I have no idea if a copy can be found on Compuserve; UNIXFORUM might<br>  have it, if any forum does.<br><br>* As far as MSDOS is concerned, the 1542A and the 1542B controllers are<br>  the same; with MSDOS, the 1542A should work as well as the 1542B.<br>  However, the hardware for these two boards is not 100% identical, and<br>  there is at least one (NON-MSDOS) program that initially did not work<br>  with a 1542A, but did work with a 1542B (BSD386 -- a 386 version of<br>  BSD Unix).<br><br>* In case anyone's curious, here's an edited copy of my CONFIG.SYS file:<br><br> FILES=40<br> BUFFERS=40<br> BREAK=ON<br> STACKS=10,256<br> DEVICE=c:\sys\dev\aspi4dos.sys /d /n4<br> DEVICE=C:\QEMM\QEMM386.SYS on RAM ROM DMA=32 ST:M X=F800-FFFF<br> DOS=HIGH,UMB<br> DEVICEHIGH=c:\sys\dev\nnansi.sys<br> DEVICEHIGH=C:\DOS\SETVER.EXE<br> shell = c:\dos\command.com /p<br><br>  Note that I'm using QEMM and ASPI4DOS 3.1.  If I were using ASPI4DOS<br>  3.0 or 3.0a, I'd probably have to use a CONFIG.SYS that looked like:<br><br> FILES=40<br> BUFFERS=40<br> BREAK=ON<br> STACKS=10,256<br> DEVICE=c:\sys\dev\aspi4dos.sys /d /n4<br> DEVICE=C:\QEMM\QEMM386.SYS on RAM ROM DMA=32 ST:M X=F800-FFFF<br> DOS=HIGH,UMB<br> DEVICEHIGH=c:\sys\dev\scsiha.sys /V386<br> DEVICEHIGH=c:\sys\dev\nnansi.sys<br> DEVICEHIGH=C:\DOS\SETVER.EXE<br> shell = c:\dos\command.com /p<br><br>  If I weren't using ASPI4DOS, I'd probably use something that looked<br>  like:<br><br> FILES=40<br> BUFFERS=40<br> BREAK=ON<br> STACKS=10,256<br> DEVICE=c:\sys\dev\scsiha.sys /V386<br> DEVICE=C:\QEMM\QEMM386.SYS on RAM ROM DB=32 DMA=32 ST:M X=F800-FFFF<br> DOS=HIGH,UMB<br> DEVICEHIGH=c:\sys\dev\nnansi.sys<br> DEVICEHIGH=C:\DOS\SETVER.EXE<br> shell = c:\dos\command.com /p<br><br>  However, if I used a floppy-controller-based tape drive, or if I<br>  planned to record high-quality sound from a sound card, I would still<br>  need some way of changing the Adaptec's bus on/off times.  The first<br>  two versions of CONFIG.SYS take care of this, but this last version<br>  doesn't.<br><br>

1	Someone said that it really takes 3 clock cycles to<br>perform a transfer, so that reduces the transfer rate to 5.5MB/s,<br>which is the commonly-used figure for ISA bus speed.	Well, some quick math on my part shows that an 8.3MHz bus, 16 bits<br>wide, performing a transfer every two clock cycles will provide 8.3M<br>bytes/sec.  Someone said that it really takes 3 clock cycles to<br>perform a transfer, so that reduces the transfer rate to 5.5MB/s,<br>which is the commonly-used figure for ISA bus speed.  However, I<br>believe a two-clock transfer is possible (0 wait states).

1	Proc.	As described the manual the following steps must be done for th initialization<br>of the S3 card.<br><br>InitDisplay;<br> (*BIOS-Call<br>  with AX-Reg = 4F02H<br>  with BX-Reg = 105H for 1024 x 768 x 256 resolution<br>  Interrupt 10H *)<br>Unlock Register Lock 1 (CR38)<br> (* For access to S3 Register Set *)<br>Unlock Register Lock 2 (CR39)<br> (* For access to Syst. Control and Syst. Extension Register *)<br>Unlock Graphic Command Group (CR40)<br> (* Set Bit 0 to 1 in Syst. Configuration Register *)<br>Unlock Advanced Display Functions<br> (* Set Bit 0 to 1 in Function Control Register *)<br><br>After these operations the FIFO-stack of the S3 should be empty.<br>When we watch the status (Graph. Proc. Status), we always get<br>the value 0FH instead of 0H.<br>Full would mean 0FFH (8 places occupied), empty would mean 0H<br>(0 places occupied).<br><br>It is possible to read this register in two different ways. <br>Both times we get different results.<br><br><br><br>Our machine is a 486 DX/2 with EISA bus and a S3 86C805 local bus. <br><br>--> any ideas?

1	These are the marker holes which let the tape drive know when it is <br>at the end of the tape.	Did you open up the tape cartridge and put the tape back on the reels?<br><br>If you have not yet, open it up by removing the two screws on the bottom of<br>the tape cartridge and snap the plastic shell away from the metal base.  <br>As you are pulling the tape through the assembly try not to touch any more <br>than you have to.  As you are doing it, look for a couple of little holes in <br>the tape.  These are the marker holes which let the tape drive know when it is <br>at the end of the tape.  The holes are spaced a couple of inches apart.  My best<br>guess is that the drive finds the first marker and then stops on the second<br>marker?  <br><br>Anyhow, If the tape has the holes, then check to see if the mirror on the tape<br>is clean.  The function of the mirror is to detect the marker holes.  The<br>tape drive shines a light at the mirror and has a pickup in the area where<br>the reflection would come out.  When the hole goes by, the pickup detects the <br>light that was allowed to pass and it knows when to stop.  If the mirror is dirty<br>or out of alighment (unlikely since it is made into the case) you might have<br>a problem detecting the end of the tape.<br><br>If the tape drive does it on all tapes and the tapes all look good, then either<br>the pickup or the LED of the sensor system has failed or is dirty.  If you open<br>the drive door you will see the sensor assembly to the left of the R/W head<br>assembly.  If it looks clean and nothing is in its way, then the drive may<br>need to be serviced.  It is possible that the LED is burned out or the sensor<br>is out. <br><br>If it is still in warranty, you might be able to send it back to CMS for<br>repair.<br><br><br>---<br>Scott Hiles<br>whiles@relay.nswc.navy.mil
1	The floppy, on the other hand, is not buffered, so that using the CPU<br>for floppy data transfer (as was done on the PC Jr, by the way) really<br>does stink.	Actually, it does make a reasonable amount of sense. Fixed disk<br>sectors are buffered by the controller, and transferring them to<br>memory with a 'rep insw' (or whatever the instruction is called) is<br>quite efficient (single instruction, goes as fast as the<br>controller/cpu know how to use the bus). Since the 286 wasn't cached,<br>the bus is likely a critical resource relative to CPU performance, and<br>it's possible that DMA bus interference would cause as much or more<br>loss of CPU cycles (for 'computing') as does the 'rep insw' sequence.<br><br>The floppy, on the other hand, is not buffered, so that using the CPU<br>for floppy data transfer (as was done on the PC Jr, by the way) really<br>does stink.
1	DOS<br>just sits and waits anyway.	The Adaptec 1540-series use bus mastering.  This means that the CPU<br>doesn't sit waiting for data bytes, it can go off and do other<br>computing - if you have an advanced multi-tasking OS, that is.  DOS<br>just sits and waits anyway.<br><br><br>Why don't you start with the spec-sheet of the ISA bus first? :-) IDE<br>was designed to plug into ISA virtually unaided - in essence, IDE *is*<br>ISA, on a ribbon cable.  Therefore it's specs are the same as ISA -<br>8MHz clock, 16 bit width, 5MB/sec.<br><br>This is why I've concluded that IDE on VL-bus is a waste of a fast<br>slot.  The card's job would to slow the VL-bus transactions to ISA<br>speed.  Heck, that's what ISA slots do - I'll just use one of those<br>instead.

1	A controller that allows reselection can operate<br>even better with multiple devices.	The Amiga Zorro II bus is comparable with the ISA bus (7.16 vs 8.33 MHZ).<br>The Amiga has had a pre-emptative multi-tasking  OS since '85 and can<br>operate with 1 MB RAM!  SCSI is used almost exclusively on these systems.<br><br>A SCSI controller that transfers data by DMA allows the cpu to request data<br>from the hard drive and continue working while the controller gets the data<br>and moves it to memory.  A controller that allows reselection can operate<br>even better with multiple devices.  This is espically true with SCSI tape<br>units.  For example, when rewinding or formatting a tape, the command is<br>issued to the controller and the bus is released to allow access to other<br>devices on the bus.  This greatly increases productivity or, at least, do<br>something else while backing up your hard drive :-).  Which happens to be<br>what I am doing while reading this group.<br><br>Its a long story, but I still use IDE on my 486 except for the CDROM which,<br>thanks to SCSI, I can move between both machines.  If, and when, SCSI is<br>better standardized and supported on the ibm-clone machines, I plan to<br>completely get rid of IDE.<br>--<br>  Wayne Summer        //        AMIGA - Simply the Best.<br>  Palm Bay, FL. US  \X/        wayne@amtower.spacecoast.org

0	Everyone who is a registered user of C7 should have received a<br>considerable amount of info regarding the specifics of C7.	VC++ *is* considered an upgrade for C7.  There will be no product<br>called C 8.0 (although the command-line compiler of VC++ lists its<br>version as 8.00).  C7 is not a "DOS"-only product -- it is a C/C++<br>compiler capable of producing executables for DOS or Windows, as is<br>VC++ (Pro. Ed.).  The (significant) difference is that VC++ comes with<br>many Windows-hosted tools (ide, etc) which assist developers in<br>creating Windows executables much more quickly (basically by<br>automating the thousands of lines of boilerplate code).  Initial<br>impressions have been very favorable.<br><br>Everyone who is a registered user of C7 should have received a<br>considerable amount of info regarding the specifics of C7. 0	I am sure there are other problems along the same lines, but I have<br>not encountered them (yet).	I have never gotten the _entire_ ctrltest app to run under BC++, but<br>the reason is that MS makes some bad assumptions about the order in<br>which static/global objects are initialized (i.e. some objects are<br>getting accessed before they are initialized).  The problem is in the<br>owner-draw menu code somewhere-- if you comment out that section, all<br>other pieces of ctrltest work fine.<br><br>Two other major gotchas I have found using MFC under BC++:<br><br>- The CFile::OpenFlags enum uses hard-coded numbers for the open mode,<br>  rather than the manifest constants defined in fcntrl.h (which differ<br>  between MSC and BC).<br><br>- All of the MFC collection classes depend on another bad C++<br>  assumption-- that a reference to a base object can used be in place<br>  of a reference to a derived object (true for pointers, NOT for<br>  references).<br><br>I am sure there are other problems along the same lines, but I have<br>not encountered them (yet).  I have not seen MFC 2.0 yet, but I hope<br>that some of these will be addressed.  If they are not, all of MS's<br>hype about portability to other vendor's compilers will be just that.<br>

1	I think we go to AT LEAST 76 Hz at 1024x768x8, and maybe more (and<br>it's a function of the RAMDAC speed, not the Power 9000).	This is not the case: the ROM on the P9000 supports VESA modes of up to<br>1024x768 in 256 colors.  VESA-compliant applications should have no trouble<br>setting these modes. (But I'm forwarding your posting to our Software group,<br>just in case.  Can't be too careful.)  Not that I doubt that YOUR applications<br>are failing to run; lots of stuff depends on figuring out which exact SVGA<br>they're looking at, and don't use VESA calls (VESA is still pretty new).<br>Every new chip set confuses them.<br><br><br>I think we go to AT LEAST 76 Hz at 1024x768x8, and maybe more (and<br>it's a function of the RAMDAC speed, not the Power 9000). We need to<br>fix the problems you've noted (they were already on the list).  If<br>you're really interested, though, take a look at the text file<br>P9000RES.DAT, which holds the data from which the choices in the<br>P9000 monitor installation program are built.  Working by analogy,<br>you can build up a new monitor definition that has the right<br>combinations of refresh rates for your monitors.  Keep a backup copy<br>of the file!  Once you've built a new version of the P9000RES.DAT<br>file, run the P9000 installation program, INST, and your new choices<br>should show up.  (This assumes you have the WEITEK v. 2.2 drivers.<br>You can tell the rev number by looking at the modification time of<br>the driver: 02:20 is version 2.20.  Microsoft uses this gimmick,<br>too.)<br><br><br>The SPEEDY benchmark was put out by Hercules and IIT, who to my<br>knowledge were unencumbered by any motivations except making the<br>Hercules Graphite/IIT AGX014 card look really good.  So I'd take the<br>numbers with a ton of salt. (Texas Instruments did the same thing<br>with WINTACH, trying to make the 34020 look good compared to the<br>8514, as if anyone cared.)  It's safer (though not safe) to use<br>benchmarks from "unbiased" sources, such as testing labs, columnists,<br>etc.<br><br><br><br>I think you'll a large discrepancy between the results of SPEEDY and<br>the results of anything else in the universe on these things.<br><br><br>Font caching is a perfectly legitimate optimization -- Windows has<br>hooks for it built right into the GDI.  What's kind of silly is IIT's<br>use of a hardwired "The quick brown fox jumped over the lazy dog then<br>sat on a tack" string in their driver.  Not only is it useless in<br>real applications, it lacks the programming elegance of the "Bart<br>Simpson optimization," in which you save the bitmap of the<br>most-recently drawn string in off-screen memory, and just do a<br>screen-to-screen bitblit if you happen to be given that same string a<br>second time in a row.  (We call it the "Bart Simpson optimization"<br>because Bart's the only person we can see benefiting from it: he<br>could right "I will not cheat on benchmarks" a hundred times and be<br>done in half the time it would take to actually form each character.)<br><br><br>We don't have any lawyers -- they're all working for Intel.  There<br>used to be a lawyer in Montana who didn't, but he died.<br><br> -- Robert<br><br>

1	With I/O overlap, your CPU can continue to "think" while disk<br>operations are underway, whereas without overlap, your CPU sits idly<br>waiting for each disk operation to finish - and disk operations take<br>an *eternity*, compared to a fast CPU.	There is a way in which a multi-tasking computer actually gives you<br>more CPU power then you had before, and that is with I/O overlap.<br>With I/O overlap, your CPU can continue to "think" while disk<br>operations are underway, whereas without overlap, your CPU sits idly<br>waiting for each disk operation to finish - and disk operations take<br>an *eternity*, compared to a fast CPU.<br><br>So, when you've got multi-tasking, you want to increase performance by<br>increasing the amount of overlapping you do.<br><br>One way is with DMA or bus mastering.  Either of these make it<br>possible for I/O devices to move their data into and out of memory<br>without interrupting the CPU.  The alternative is for the CPU to move<br>the data.  There are several SCSI interface cards that allow DMA and<br>bus mastering.  IDE, however, is defined by the standard AT interface<br>created for the IBM PC AT, which requires the CPU to move all the data<br>bytes, with no DMA.

0	Microsoft<br>admits to a problem with older versions of the PostScript printer driver,<br>but I've found it to be pretty generic.	This is a common problem with highly complex TrueType fonts.  Microsoft<br>admits to a problem with older versions of the PostScript printer driver,<br>but I've found it to be pretty generic.<br><br>You can get around the problem by adjusting the parameter OutlineThreshold<br>in the [TrueType] section of WIN.INI.  This entry specifies the number of<br>pels-per-em at which Windows will render TrueType fonts as outline fonts<br>instead of as bitmap fonts.  The default is 256.  I've generally been able<br>to get fonts to work by setting OutlineThreshold=160.  Depending on your<br>printer resolution and the point size you are using, you may need a<br>different value.<br><br>The Windows Resource Kit warns against going above 300.  Presumably, that<br>might cause fonts to print as square boxes or something.  :-|  (I'm not<br>smiling.)<br>

1	This eliminates ambaguity.	Ok once again with the SCSI spec list:<br>SCSI-1 {with a SCSI-1 controler chip} synchronous range is indeed 0-5MB/s<br> asynchronous range is slower at 0-3MB/s.<br>SCSI-1 {With a SCSI-2 controller chip}: 4-6MB/s with 10MB/s burst {8-bit}<br> Note the INCREASE in SPEED, the Mac Quadra uses this version of SCSI-1<br> so it DOES exist. Some PCs use this set up too.<br><br>SCSI-2 {8-bit/SCSI-1 mode}:          4-6MB/s with 10MB/s burst<br>{for those who want SCSI-2 but don't want to pay for the 16-bit or 32-bit<br> hardware or mess with the SCSI-2 software controllers.  Usable by SCSI-1<br> devices with close to 8-bit SCSI-2 speeds}<br>SCSI-2 {16-bit/wide or fast mode}:  8-12MB/s with 20MB/s burst<br>SCSI-2 {32-bit/wide AND fast}:     15-20MB/s with 40MB/s burst<br><br>On the other interfaces let DXB132@psuvm.psu.edu speak:<br>Again synchronous and asynchronous modes with asynchronous much slower<br> {Range 0-5MB/s}<br><br>One problem is the inconsitant use of the term 'SCSI' in articles and by<br>people.  Its PROPER meaning is "The set of SCSI interfaces composed of<br>SCSI-1 AND SCSI-2"<br>Look at the inconsitant use of SCSI in the below quote:<br>(My comments in {})<br><br>PC Magazine April 27, 1993:29 "Although SCSI is twice as fast as ESDI,<br>{This is asynchronous SCSI-1 with a SCSI-1 chip} 20% faster than IDE..."<br>{this is BOTH asynchronous SCSI-1 with a SCSI-2 chip AND 8-bit SCSI-2}<br>To read CONSITANTLY the quote SHOULD read:<br>{asynchronous SCSI-1 with a SCSI-1 chip}<br>"Although asynchronous SCSI-1 is twice as fast as ESDI, one third the <br>speed of IDE..."<br>or {asynchronous SCSI-1 with a SCSI-2 chip or 8-bit SCSI-2}<br>"Although SCSI-1 with a SCSI-2 chip and 8-bit SCSI-2 are eight times as fast as<br>ESDI, 20% faster than IDE..."<br><br>NOTE the NONUSE of 'SCSI' by itself.  This eliminates ambaguity.<br><br>SCSI-1 drivers are somewhat reasonable while 16-bit and 32-bit SCSI-2 drivers <br>are VERY expansive {8-bit SCSI-2 can use SCSI-1 drivers with little speed<br>degridation(the Mac Quadra does EXACTLY this.)}
0	In the last PC-Magazine they benchmarked some of the new accelerators,<br>and admitted that many of them 'cheated' on WinBench.	And why they are considering using WinBench as a test, they may want<br>to read everything Steve Gibson has said on the subject in his<br>InfoWorld column the past couple of month.  In short, virtually<br>every board manufacturer cheats on the test by writing things in the<br>driver that is there only to make the board appear faster on the<br>WinBench suite.  So the WinBench score has no bearing in reality to<br>how cards stack up on real world tasks.<br><br>In the last PC-Magazine they benchmarked some of the new accelerators,<br>and admitted that many of them 'cheated' on WinBench.  Interestingly,<br>they 'allowed' one type of cheating behaviour (caching for bitblt<br>operations, no matter how unreal), yet did not allow a couple of other<br>types of cheating behaviour that some of the cards used.  At least<br>one card was eliminated from the "Editor's Choice" because of<br>cheating on the benchmark.

1	MODEL      CYLINDERS   HEADS    SECTORS   CAPACITY (Million bytes)<br><br>M2622T      1013        10        63         326.75<br>M2623T      1002        13        63         420.16<br>M2624T      995         16        63         513.51<br><br><br>There are 6 switches on the switch block on these drives.	<br>last update: 14.4.1993<br><br>collected by Carsten Grammes (ph12hucg@rz.uni-sb.de)<br>and published regularly on comp.sys.ibm.pc.hardware.<br><br><br>!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!<br>There is explicitly NO WARRANTY<br>that the given settings are correct or harmless. (I only collect, I do<br>not check for myself!!!). There is always the possibility that the<br>settings may destroy your hardware!<br>!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!<br><br><br>Since I hope however that only well-minded people undergo the effort of<br>posting their settings the chance of applicability exists. If you should<br>agree or disagree with some setting, let me know immediately in order<br>to update the list.<br><br>If you possess a HD not mentioned here of which you know BIOS and/or<br>jumper settings, please mail them to me for the next update of the list!<br><br>Only IDE (AT-Bus) Harddisks will be accounted for.<br>If not specified the Landing Zone should be set equal to the number of<br>cylinders. If not specified the 'Write Precompensation' should be set<br>65535. (There are BIOSes that don't even ask for it).<br><br>Another statement (maybe right):<br>IDE harddisk don't have Precomp and LZone. The precomp is a built-in parameter<br>and lzone isn't used because most if not every IDE disk has autopark.<br><br>The jumpers' names are given as printed on the HD's board, often only a<br>jumper number (JP12 means the jumper '12'). A zero means that the jumper<br>is left open, a one means that the jumper is closed.<br><br><br><br>***************************   C O N N E R   ***************************<br><br><br>  Conner Peripherals Drive Geometry<br><br>IDE/AT<br><br>Conner drives are low level formatted at the factory. closed<br><br><br>Maxtor 7213A<br><br>Default (power-up) AT BIOS Translation Parameters (others possible)<br>Cyl   Hds  SpT  MBytes<br>683   16   38   212<br><br>There are two sets of jumpers. A set of 5 and a set of<br>4. With the power and IDE connector toward you, the set of 5 is<br>numbered (left to right) J16 - J20  , and the set of 4 is numbered<br>(bottom to top) J22-J25. <br><br>The only jumper of normal interest is J20. Jumper it for only<br>drive in a single drive system, or master drive in a dual drive<br>system.<br> <br>Remove the jumper J20 for slave drive in a dual drive system. <br><br>J19 is a dummy and may be used to store the spare shunt if the <br>drive is configured for a slave mode.<br><br>Jumpers J17, J18, J24, J25 are factory reserved. Abnormal operation<br>may occur if jumpered.<br><br>Jumper 22 is sync spindle enabled/disabled  (open=disabled)<br>Jumper 23 is sync slave/master              (open=slave)<br>Jumper 16 is I/O Channel Ready              (open=disabled)<br><br><br>Maxtor 7245A (245Mb IDE; self-parking; Universal Translate):<br>Drive type : User defineable<br>Cyl    Hds    WPC    LZ     S/T<br>967    16     0      0 master with slave, DS or, DS and SP in some cases<br> slave, no strap<br><br><br>***************************   R O D I M E    *********************************<br><br>Information for RO 3008A and RO 3009A series hard disk drives:<br><br>Drive Types<br><br>   Model Cyls Hds Sectors/Trk  No. blocks Formatted Cap.<br>  ------- ---- --- -----------  ---------- --------------<br>  RO3058A 868  3     34     88,536  45.33 MByets<br>  RO3088A 868  5     34    147,560  75.55 MByets<br>  RO3095A 923  5     34    156,910  80.33 MByets<br>  RO3128A 868  7     34    206,584 105.77 MByets<br>  RO3135A 923  7     34    219,674 112.47 MByets<br> <br>  RO3059A 217 15     28     91,158  46.67 MByets<br>  RO3089A 325 15     28    136,737  70.00 MByets<br>  RO3129A 492 15     28    206,645 105.80 MByets<br>  RO3139A 523 15     28    219,735 112.50 MByets<br>  RO3209A 759 15     28    319,053 163.35 MByets<br>  RO3259A 976 15     28    410,211 210.02 MByets<br><br><br>Link Options<br><br>   In order to install the Rodime Ro 3000A series drives the dumpers for <br>the single/dual drive and LED operation on the drive need to be set as <br>described in the relevant product specification.<br>   I a single drive environment the drive is described as a Master.<br>   In a dual drive environment the drives are described as a Master and a<br>Slave. This is due to the protocal the takes place between the two drives <br>when performing diagnostics.<br>   There are four links, LK1, LK2, LK4 and LK5, adjacent to the 40 way <br>interface connector. They have the following functions and are described <br>in order as viewed from the end of the drive, with the first jumper <br>described nearest the 40 way interface connector.<br><br>LK2: LED <br>     When fitted, this jumper connects the LED drive to pin 39 of the<br>     interface. This allows a LED to be connected to the interface. An<br>     external current limiting resistor needs to be fitted in series with<br>     the LED when this option is selected. The value of the resistor will<br>     be dependant on the LED type chosen but will be in the range of 130<br>     Ohms ot 220 Ohms.<br><br>LK1: Dual Drives<br>     This jumper must be fitted when two drives are attached to a single<br>     bus. It fallows communication across the 40 way interface connector,<br>     indicating, to the Master drive, the presence of a Slave.<br><br>LK4: Master<br>     When fitted this signifies that the drive jumpered is a Master. If<br>     there are two drives connected on a single bus then only one may be<br>     jumpered in this way.<br><br>LK5: IOChRdy<br>     When fitted this connects the IOChRdy signal to the drive, it is <br>     fitted when the drive is used in host systems that have a higher<br>     data transfer rate than the drive i.e. greater than 4 MBytes per<br>     second when using 1:1 interleave. This jumper is not normally <br>     fitted as most hosts transfer at a lower rate than 4 MBytes per<br>     second.<br><br>   There are four possible Master/Slave configurations in which a drive(s)<br>may be jumpered:<br><br>     Master, single drive with LED on interface  LK2 & LK4 fitted.<br>     Master, single drive without LED on interface LK4 only fitted.<br>     Master, dual drive without LED on interface LK4 & LK1 fitted.<br>     Slave, dual drive without LED on interface  No jumpers fitted.<br>     Master, dual drive with LED on interface  LK4, LK1 & LK2 fitted.<br>     Slave, dual drive with LED on interface  LK2 only fitted.<br><br>   The Master drive will delay power-up for approximately two seconds to<br>reduce power surges in applications where dual drives are used.<br><br>   The other connections for a LED will be found close to the 28 way <br>connector at the other end of the drive. This LED driver is not affected<br>by the link options. An internal current limiting resistor is on the <br>drive for this LED driver. Refer to the product specification for further<br>details.<br><br><br><br><br>***************************   S E A G A T E   *************************<br><br>There is a list of most Seagate HD (including MFM, SCSI, ESDIand IDE) on<br>every Simtel mirror under<br><br>/msdos/dskutl/1seagate.zip<br><br>It contains info about the following drives:<br><br>     st1144a st138a     st274a st3283a<br>st1057a     st1156a st1400a     st280a st351ax<br>st1090a     st1162a st1401a     st3051a st9051a<br>st1102a     st1186a st1480a     st3096a st9077a<br>st1111a     st1201a st157a     st3120a st9096a<br>st1126a     st1239a st2274a     st3144a st9144a<br>st1133a     st125a st2383a     st325ax<br><br><br>********************* T E A C   **************<br><br>Model: SD-3105<br><br>                Cyls. 0	Otherwise, the default value is the /e: option in the<br>SHELL= command in CONFIG.SYS.	    CommandEnvSize=NNNN<br><br>"This sets the COMMAND.COM env size, where NNNN must either be 0 or<br>between 160 and 32768.  A value of 0 disables the setting.  If this<br>value is too small or too big, it is rounded up to 160 or down to 32768.<br>If the value is less than the current size of the actual environment,<br>this setting is disabled, as if it were 0.  If you specify the<br>environment size in a PIF file for COMMAND.COM, the PIF setting<br>overrides this setting.  The default is 0 with MSDOS versions earlier<br>than 3.2.  Otherwise, the default value is the /e: option in the<br>SHELL= command in CONFIG.SYS.  To set this value, you must edit your<br>SYSTEM.INI [and reboot]."<br><br><br>I have used this entry, as well as relied on the default /e: from<br>the CONFIG.SYS shell= line, and both give larger environments.  If<br>you don't use one of these, then the environment passed by windows<br>to each new DOS box is just a little bit bigger than the environment<br>_variables_ present when windows was started.  (No matter how big<br>the DOS env was, when windows starts, it truncates all unused space<br>except for a few bytes.)  This should allow your batch file to run,<br>but your mileage may vary.<br>

0	If any changes are required, or a new version is released, complete<br>   the above procedures again.	WSM is a compilation of submissions from shareware/freeware authors in a single<br>Windows .HLP (Help System) file.  All types of software may be submitted for<br>entry in WSM - utilities, applications, games, programming tools, etc.<br><br>WSM benefits Windows shareware/freeware authors by allowing them to publicize<br>their software releases, inform users of updated versions, and to increase<br>their installed base of users.  Those searching for specific Windows software<br>will benefit by having a complete list of available software available at their<br>fingertips - complete with feature lists, current prices, and any other<br>relevant information.<br><br>Windows software authors may submit entries to Windows Shareware Monthly in the<br>following manner:<br><br>1. Compose a short summary of the function of the software.  Include all<br>   special features which are unique to your product and which set it apart<br>   from other programs in the genre.  Be sure to include specific details<br>   such as: the current release version, where the software is available,<br>   how much the registration fee costs, how much disk space is required, any<br>   special requirements (e.g. sound card, or VBRUN200.DLL, etc.), how the<br>   author may be contacted, etc.  Because text is highly compressible, the<br>   summary may be as long as is necessary, however, it is best to keep it<br>   short.  A good guideline is a single screenful of 12-point text at 640x480<br>   resolution.  Order forms and other such addendum may be included if desired.<br>   All submissions whould be in plain text (ASCII) format.  Formatting will<br>   be exactly as it is submitted - I will simply cut and paste text files<br>   into a Help Authoring system.  If you require special formatting <br>   conventions - such as boldface text or italics, or a larger font size,<br>   indicate so CLEARLY within the text file.  For best results, use Windows<br>   NotePad to create the .TXT file. <br> <br>2. Include up to 100k of Windows-format .BMP (bitmap) screen-shots which<br>   display the workings or special features of each program.  Special cases<br>   (rendering applications for example) which require 256-color bitmaps may<br>   submit up to 250k of .BMP files - all other should observe the 100k limit<br>   and use 16-color format. 1	As far as I know, the "speed buffering"<br>works OK for external modems if a 550 is used on the internal serial<br>port board.	However, I can tell you to stay clear of any board which uses the<br>Rockwell MPU (as opposed to the DPU) for an internal implementation.<br>This is because the MPU used "speed buffering" instead of having a<br>16550 interface.  Without the 550 interface, the number of interrupts<br>are still the same and thus may get dropped under multitasking<br>conditions (like in windows).  As far as I know, the "speed buffering"<br>works OK for external modems if a 550 is used on the internal serial<br>port board.<br><br>Hope this helps...<br>Tom<br>

1	JimL<br>--------------------------------------------------------------------<br><br>-- <br>Mailer address is buggy!	Your machine will run at whatever the bus is jumpered to/CMOS is set to<br>(usually wait states) regardless of what speed RAM is installed.  No<br>motherboard can sense the speed of the RAM installed, unless you call<br>failing as a sort of auto-sense.  This is how you can sometimes use<br>"slower" RAM in a machine.  You either set the number of wait states to<br>accomodate the slow RAM (in which case, all memory will run at that<br>slower rate) or you reduce the wait states and take the chance that the<br>slower RAM will act like faster RAM and you won't crash.<br><br>Putting faster RAM in won't speed things up unless you tell the machine<br>it has faster RAM.  <br><br>Mixing fast and slow RAM will not help you if you have to keep the bus <br>slowed down to accomodate slow RAM.<br><br>JimL<br>--------------------------------------------------------------------<br><br>-- <br>Mailer address is buggy!  Reply to: jiml@strauss.FtCollinsCO.NCR.com

0	Try to locate the UART on your mother board, multi-function I/O card, COM<br>board or ISA/MCA modem card.	re: What to do after the high speed modem arrives.       Edition 01 Apr 93<br><br>This article applies only to Windows 3.0 and 3.1, and not to NT, OS/2<br>or DOS apps not running under Windows.<br><br>One of the unadvertised limitations of most current Windows PCs is that<br>their RS-232C (serial, COM) performance is seriously deficient.  Almost<br>everyone who purchases a high-speed modem (V.32bis, V.32, PEP or HST)<br>discovers the problem the first time they try to download a file or accept<br>an incoming FAX (at 9600+) after upgrading the modem.  Overrun and retry<br>errors abound, even when the only active application is the datacomm or<br>FAX program.  If the transfer completes at all, it may take even longer<br>than with the old 2400bps modem.<br><br><br>There are three reasons for the problem:<br><br>1. The Universal Asynchronous Receiver/Transmitters (UARTs) used in most<br>   PCs are primitive Ns8250 devices with single-byte FIFO buffers.  If the<br>   operating system/driver cannot read and flush each character at high<br>   interrupt rates, the next incoming character overwrites the FIFO and<br>   the previous one is lost.  DOS, being a fairly single-minded<br>   environment during datacomm, can usually keep up. Windows can't.<br><br>2. Windows has more operating system overhead than plain DOS, and<br>   interrupts often take longer to service.  Overruns are much more likely<br>   than under DOS.  As soon as you report to your PC/modem vendor that you<br>   are losing data, you may be advised that "you need to upgrade to a<br>   16550". More likely, since there seems to be a conspiracy of ignorance<br>   about this issue,  you'll get no useful advice at all.  Most of the<br>   store-front and mail-order sources I spoke with in attempting to solve<br>   my own problem had never heard the term "16550" and many didn't even<br>   know what a UART was.<br><br>3. Even your PC has Ns16550A UARTs (and PS/2's do), or if you can upgrade<br>   your mother/COM board or add a new COM board, you may STILL experience<br>   errors and overruns because the standard MicroSoft Windows COM drivers<br>   don't take full advantage of the 16550.  Windows 3.1 is improved in this<br>   regard over 3.0, but I still recommend a driver upgrade.  Applications<br>   like ProComm+/Win (which is what I use) cannot get around this problem<br>   by themselves.<br><br>If you have a modem CARD, you may not have a problem, as the modem part of<br>the card can be designed to be aware of the state of the UART, and avoid<br>overrunning it; however, I wouldn't want to bet that the card designers<br>were that clever, and will insist on a 16550 UART if I ever buy a modem<br>card.  Some modem cards don't even have conventional UARTs, but if they<br>are to work with standard Windows drivers, they need to simulate one.<br>Use MSD.EXE (below) to see what the modem card is, or is pretending to be.<br><br><br>The Hardware Situation.<br><br>The UARTs on most PC COM ports are based on National Semiconductor Ns8250<br>or Ns16450 chips (or megacells inside VLSI chips where you can't replace<br>them).  You can ID the UART type on your system by running the MicroSoft<br>diagnostic program \WINDOWS\MSD.EXE.  Be sure to run it in DOS *before*<br>bringing up Windows.   The Windows serial API may prevent MSD from<br>accurately identifying a 16550 if you run it from a Windows DOS prompt.<br><br>The Ns16550 UART has separate 16-byte transmit and receive FIFOs with<br>configurable trigger levels, and can run reliably at clock rates up to<br>460,800 bps, although with current modem technology, there's no point in<br>pushing your luck by going over 115,200 bps. The 16550 has shorted access<br>cycle times than the 16450 or 8250.  The 16550 also has DMA capability, but<br>it is not clear that any PC drivers ever use this.  For more technical info,<br>see National Semiconductor Application Note AN-491.<br><br>So, what UART component do you have?<br><br>Try to locate the UART on your mother board, multi-function I/O card, COM<br>board or ISA/MCA modem card.  If you can't find a socketed component with<br>the numbers "8250" or "16450", your COM ports are probably buried in VLSI,<br>and you won't be able to perform a chip replacement.  If you can DISABLE<br>your VLSI COM ports (as I chose to do), you can at least add an aftermarket<br>COM board.<br><br>If you have one or more socketed 8250 or 16450 chips, you can buy plug-in<br>Ns16550AFN or PC16C550CN (low power CMOS version) ICs from several<br>suppliers typically for $9 to $15 each.  The "N" chip is the normal 40-pin<br>dual-in-line package.  Other styles are available, but avoid any Ns16550<br>chips without the "A" (the 16C550C are presumably all OK).<br><br>Early Ns chips have bugs, although National will reportedly exchange those<br>of their own manufacture for free.  Clone chips are available from various<br>IC makers other than National.  The manual for the TurboCom drivers states<br>support for the following (apparently equivalent) chips:<br>National Semiconductor: 16550A, 16551, 16552<br>Chips&Technology:       82C607<br>Texas Instruments:      t16c550a<br>Silicon Systems:        73M550<br>VLSI                    16C550<br>TurboCom warns about the pre-"A" Ns16550 and Western Digital 16C550,<br>says that problems have been reported with early IBM PS/2 55SX and 70<br>systems (IBM reportedly will upgrade them).<br><br>If you DON'T have socketed 8250/16450 chips, you'll need to buy an after-<br>market COM or multi-function board. If this is a modem card situation, you<br>may be hosed. To add a new COM or multi-function card, you'll need to<br>either disable the COM1/2 port(s) you are replacing, or re-assign them to<br>COM3/4 (although watch out for IRQ conflicts without TurboCom).<br><br>Although cheaper cards are available, in the interest of getting the<br>problem solved quickly I elected buy the Modular Circuit Technology<br>MCT-AIO+ card from:<br><br>JDR Microdevices<br>2233 Samaritan Drive<br>San Jose  CA  95124<br>(800) 538-5000 voice US<br>(408) 559-1200 voice other<br>(800) 538-5005 FAX US<br><br>The MCT-AIO+ (and the "+" is important) sells for $89.95.  It is an 8-bit<br>ISA card providing:<br><br>Port Type  Connector  Address and IRQ        Comments<br>COM        DB9M       COM 1,2,3 IRQ 2,3,4,5  Ns16550AFN in socket<br>COM        ribbon     COM 2,3,4 IRQ 2,3,4,5  Ns16550AFN in socket<br>Parallel   DB25F      LPT1,2,3  IRQ 5,7<br>Game       ribbon<br><br>The kit includes a ribbon cable and DB25F connector for the secondary COM<br>port, a ribbon cable/connector for the game port, two bulkhead plates for<br>the ribbon-based connectors and a 9F-to-25F adaptor cable.  Each port can<br>be individually disabled, and the COM ports have TX, RX, RTS, CTS, DTR,<br>DCD, and DSR jumpers.<br><br>JDR also sells a Super-I/O m-f card that also has IDE.<br><br>I have heard from several people about less expensive m-f I/O cards<br>with 16550s:<br><br>TSD Systems<br>(407) 331-9130<br>$19.95 for the card, plus $9.95 per 16550.<br><br>Greenfield Trading and Distributors<br>(518) 271-2473 (voice), (518) 271-7811(FAX).<br>Their card is $33 w/one 16550, $45 w/2, and they sell 16550AFNs for $13.<br><br>R&S DATA SYSTEMS, INC.<br>820 East Highway 434<br>Longwood, FL  32750<br>PHONE: (407) 331-1424<br>FAX: (407) 331-8606<br>2COM/LPT/Game card w/2 16550s for $39<br><br>I have no personal experience with any of the firms except JDR.<br><br>Meanwhile, back at the MCT card from JDR... I only needed two serial ports,<br>and am running out of IRQs on my PC, so I disabled my built-in VLSI-based<br>8250 ports. However, with the TurboCom driver (below), I could have set<br>the internals as COM3 and 4, using IRQ sharing.<br><br><br>The Software Situation<br><br>Simply upgrading to 16550 UARTs will not completely solve common overrun<br>problems.  The standard MS serial drivers don't take full advantage of the<br>16550. The Windows 3.0 drivers are even less capable, and the Windows 3.1<br>drivers have the following limitations:<br> - They enable only the receive FIFO, and only at rates above 2400 bps.<br> - They never enable the transmit FIFO, which  results in an interrupt<br>   rate of 10x during uploads.<br> - They set the trigger level at 14 bytes (too high - it's easy for 2<br>   more bytes to arrive before the driver can read the FIFO).<br> - The Ports menu of the Control Panel only allows speeds up to 19200.<br>   With a V.32bis modem,  sparse data and text can easily compress 3:1<br>   or more, suggesting that a host DTE connect rate of 57,600 bps would<br>   be effective.<br> - The API won't accept rates above "CBR_128000".<br> - The API won't let DOS programs know there is a 16550 there, and even<br>   if it did, DOS programs that aren't 16550-aware get little benefit<br>   from a 16550 port with the standard drivers.<br> - They don't allow IRQ sharing for COM3,4.<br> - The BIOS doesn't initialize COM3,4 properly in many systems.<br> - Windows provides no workaround for apps that don't provide port<br>   speed options above 19200 bps.<br><br>These problems are reportedly NOT solved in Windows NT or DOS 6.0, and may<br>or may not be addressed in any Windows releases after 3.1 (but before 4.0).<br>Rumors suggest they "may" be solved in Windows "4.0".<br><br>You can get replacement drivers that solve all of those problems by buying<br>a copy of "TurboCom", current version 1.2, from:<br><br>Bio-Engineering Research<br>Pacific CommWare Division<br>180 Beacon Hill Lane<br>Ashland  OR   97520-9701<br>(503) 482-2744 voice<br>(503) 482-2627 FAX<br>(503) 482-2633 BBS<br>MCImail:    344-5374<br>CompuServe: 71521,760<br><br>Price was around $50 as I recall.  Bio-Eng is not set up to accept credit<br>cards, so I had to send a check.  Egghead and 1-800-Software list TurboCom<br>but as far as I know, they don't stock it.  Bio is not a software company<br>per se.  They apparently needed reliable hi-speed serial connections for<br>an in-house instrument application, wrote their own driver, discovered a<br>market for it, revised it to be a general purpose COM driver suite. They<br>recently upgraded it for Windows 3.1.  It is run-time licensed.<br><br>I now have my host (DTE) connect rate set to 57,600 bps most of  my<br>datacomm apps, and I am having ZERO problems with downloads. I routinely<br>see transfer rates that exceed 2,000 bps. I am also using 115,200 bps<br>when linking an HP95LX to my PC, with lossless bi-directional I/O.<br>Uploads to various remote systems are another matter, because many hosts<br>are still using antique UARTs and drivers.<br><br>Note that 19200 is still the highest rate that the Windows 3.1 Port menu<br>in Control Panel will allow in configuring a COM port.  TurboCom gets<br>around this by allowing you to specify, on each port, a factor that will<br>set the real UART rate to a multiple of the rate passed through the<br>Windows APIs and dialog boxes.<br><br>I also have CTS/RTS hardware flow control enabled, and I suggest that you<br>do the same.  Even if you only ever transfer 7-bit ASCII data, Xon/XOff is<br>not a sufficiently reliable method of flow control.  The informal (DEC)<br>standard for Xon/Xoff hysteresis is that the sender may transmit another<br>16 (yes, sixteen) bytes after receipt of the Xoff from the receiving<br>system or device.  The 16 byte FIFO in the 16550 is clearly not big enough<br>to let us rely exclusively on Xon/Xoff.  A well-respected datacomm<br>engineer has informed me that the 16550's can't really do CTS/RTS all<br>by itself in the DOS/Windows environment, so using data rates above<br>115,200 may still be risky.<br><br>Even with hardware flow control, a 16550 with TurboCom can still<br>experience overruns in very busy systems, with lots of apps running and<br>serious swapping in progress. If this is your situation, you may need to<br>buy a co-processed COM board, but this will cost you more than a<br>16550/TurboCom upgrade.  A review of two such boards, and a review of<br>TurboCom, can be found in the Feb'93 issue of "Windows Sources" magazine.<br>I suggest trying a 16550/TurboCom upgrade first, and experiment with<br>process priorities and time slices if you are a "power user" whose<br>thrashing system still runs into comm problems.<br><br><br>Closing Soapbox Comments<br><br>The state of RS-232C serial datacomm support is an embarrassment across<br>the computer industry.  Because it is the oldest standard I/O interface,<br>the job of designing hardware and writing software often seems to be<br>assigned to the least senior or lowest ranked engineers at computer<br>companies.  The design of the average serial port is at least ten years<br>behind the state of the art. In my last job, with a major workstation<br>vendor, I lobbied for improved serial ports when they were doing the<br>initial designs of a new system.  That family of machines was<br>subsequently introduced with 16550 ports.  However, this is the<br>exception.  Few computer companies seem to have any champions for decent<br>I/O.<br><br>You may as well learn what you can about serial I/O, because this<br>situation shows no sign of improving soon.  When V.FAST arrives, I expect<br>cries of outrage from Windows users world-wide whose 8250 PCs "sort of"<br>work today with V.32, but will fail miserably with V.FAST.  Without a<br>hardware-buffered UART (like the 16550) and without software drivers that<br>use that UART to best advantage, a V.FAST modem will be a waste of money.<br><br>Regards,                                          1001-A East Harmony Road<br>Bob Niland                                        Suite 503<br>Internet:  rjn@csn.org                            Fort Collins CO 80525<br>CompuServe: 71044,2124                            (303) 223-5209<br><br>                     Copyright 1993 Robert J. Niland<br>                           All Rights Reserved<br><br>  Permission is granted for automatic redistribution of this article, via<br>  electronic, magnetic and optical media, in an unedited form, through any<br>  Usenet newsgroup where the article is posted by the author.  Permission<br>  is granted for each CompuServe and Usenet reader subscriber and each<br>  person who received this article from Compuserve, an ftp site authorized<br>  by the author or via electronic mail from the author, to retain one<br>  electronic copy and to make hardcopy reproductions of this edition of<br>  this article for personal non-commercial use, provided that no material<br>  changes are made to the article or this copyright statement. 1	LED's:   (Turbo and HD)  LED's are uni directional.	The speaker connector should have two wires going to the speaker. <br>A speaker being a coil, it's bidirectional and makes no difference<br>which way you attach.<br><br>Turbo switch.   There are three wires to control how you want turbo<br>to become active- with the switch pushed in or the switch out.    I think<br>the middle wire is common.   Use an ohm meter to figure out which wire<br>connects with the common wire when the switch is pushed in, and which two<br>wires are connected when the switch is out.   Place the appropriate<br>two wires on the turbo berg connector of the motherboard.<br><br>LED's:   (Turbo and HD)  LED's are uni directional.  Depending which<br>way the wires are attached the LED will not light.  On my AMI motherboard,<br>if the turbo switch wires are not attached to the berg connector on the<br>board, the board will power up in default in Turbo mode.   If your motherboard<br>is like that... just attace the LED wires to the board.  If the LED doesn't<br>light, power off, reverse the connectors and try again.   It should work.<br><br>If it does, then attach the turbo switch to the board.<br><br>Hope this helps.

1	But if there are already two drives, then no can do.	There is a simple answer.  If my memory serves me the scsi bios will only<br>work as the first or second drive.  Any "built-in" drives e.g. IDE are<br>installed first and then when the scsi bios runs it will try to install<br>as the next drive.  But if there are already two drives, then no can do.<br><br>The solution is simple: use the aspi4dos device driver and disable the<br>scsi bios (as it is useless in your case).  It works like a champ!  I<br>have seen a similar situation before.<br><br>Good Luck

B(>my concern is that without a complete enclosure to direct the<br>B(>cooling flow of air from the fan, "hot spots" may develop on my<br>B(>motherboard or elsewhere.<br><br>If you have an adequate supply of air moving over the system (most<br>offices or homes have positive ventilation) you can generally run a<br>system without the cover for extended periods without a problem. 0	Unless you `print' to file with the correct resolution set for the<br>final output device (image setter).	   -----Or, if you need to use a service bureau and they're only set up to use<br>   Type 1 fonts. From what I've heard (pure hearsay) the results of outputting<br>   TT fonts as Type 1 is _not_ as good as using high-quality Type 1 fonts in<br>   the first place.<br><br>Unless you `print' to file with the correct resolution set for the<br>final output device (image setter).  A problem with TT fonts in Windows<br>is that they do get converted to T1 format OK, and the hinting is even used<br>while this is done, but the resulting T1 fonts are NOT hinted.  The result<br>is that they WILL work fine on a device of the resolution assumed by the<br>printer driver when the PS file is generated, but they will not look<br>good when printed at a different resolution.
