0	i.e., comp *.	<br>No, we're not.  But we're also not going to promote pandering to<br>corporate paranoia when the real issue is convenience.  I don't *like*<br>dealing with floppies.  Personally, I have no use for changing the<br>registration info, but I see it as a valid need, and one that ought to<br>be solved using a quick little utility rather than a half-hour<br>reinstall that's just about guaranteed to mess up your settings in one<br>way or another.<br><br>So, while I'm not going to put much time into it myself, here's the<br>procedure for getting on your way to finding the encoded information:<br><br>1.  Copy all your Windows disks into the directory from which you want<br>to install it.  I've been using c:\WINSTALL myself.<br><br>2.  From there, copy that directory to something like c:\WINORIG.<br><br>3.  Install from c:\winstall.<br><br>4.  comp the two directories to determine changes.  <br>    i.e., comp *.* \winorig\*.* >\report.txt<br><br>5.  Look in the report file for the file(s) that change.  Assuming<br>they didn't cover themselves covering their own tracks, at least one<br>file should have a difference noted at a particular offset.  Locate<br>said offset in the original directory and see what's there using a hex<br>editor, and do the same for the modified one.<br><br>6.  You're on your own as far as breaking the code goes; I don't<br>really do cryptography.  It's probably just an xor key or something; I<br>think MS is more concerned with Joe Schmoe at the office personalizing<br>his copy than with "real pirates" who will probably just disassemble<br>the damned thing anyway.  This technique should work with just about<br>any obnoxious corporate product that tries to write to the original<br>floppies when you install; in some extreme cases you may not be able<br>to back the floppies up to hard disk, and will be stuck doing a<br>compare on floppies (Lotus stuff is probably like that).<br><br>As I noted before, if you can afford the space on the hard disk, and<br>don't do much in the way of customization, reinstalling from one<br>directory to another may be less arduous.  Doing some of the stuff<br>I've mentioned here may well void your license with Microsoft, as if<br>they'd ever find out.  If you aren't careful with the disk editor, you<br>could also mung something important... duh.  I guess that's a<br>disclaimer.<br><br>Have at it....<br><br>Rob

0	The problem is the LASTDRIVE command and the way NetWare in general (and<br>in this case NETX in particular) adds drives to the device chain.	First off, I haven't used W4WG (but I think that's about to change!).<br><br>The problem is the LASTDRIVE command and the way NetWare in general (and<br>in this case NETX in particular) adds drives to the device chain. <br>Setting LASTDRIVE=Z means there are no "unassigned" (as opposed to<br>"unused") drive letters for NetWare to use, as it tacks its drive<br>mappings -onto the end- of the existing list of drives.  W4WG obviously<br>attaches its network drives to "existing, unused" drive letters.<br><br>I'd guess the next thing I'd try is something like LASTDRIVE=M, which on<br>most machines will leave a fair swathe of drives for W4WG and still<br>allow up to 13 NetWare drive mappings as well.

0	You can have thousands of glyphs in a TT font<br> file (Mac/PS/Windows/Unicode char set) and use the characters<br> appropriate for the particular platform you are running the font on.	 Again, not true. The characters in a TT or Type 1 font depend on the <br> maker. If someone converts a Type 1 font to TT they'll only get the<br> characters in the font of orgin. TT allows for much more flexibility<br> in this area as well. You can have thousands of glyphs in a TT font<br> file (Mac/PS/Windows/Unicode char set) and use the characters<br> appropriate for the particular platform you are running the font on.<br><br>Well, yes, both Type 1 and TrueType fonts can contain lots of characters.<br>And in both cases plain text fonts will be reencoded to Windows ANSI<br>in Windows, and to Mac standard roman encoding on the Mac. <br>No difference there between T1 and TT!   <br><br>And as for fonts with thousands of glyphs, right know these are a liabiliaty<br>rather than a great new thing.  Reason is that a font with 3000 characters<br>is very large, and if you can only access 224 of them you are paying a heavy<br>price in downloading time and printer storage.  (And at the moment there<br>is only one program of that uses `partial font downloading')<br><br> For instance, all or our fontpack 2 TT fonts have the Mac/Windows<br> char set in them. The metrics of the fonts are such that if the font<br> is brought over to the mac the user will have access to the full mac<br> char set.  <br><br>Yes and T1 text fonts all have the `ISO Adobe' character set, which is<br>a superset of Windows ANSI and Macintosh standard roman enocding.<br><br>The question is whether you can get at the glyphs.  In Windows, for example,<br>you cannot get at the ligatures `fi' anfd `fl' (both T1 and TT)even though a<br>font may very well have them.<br><br>ANd, while it is somewhat painful to reencode a Type 1 font and to create a<br>new Windows metric file (PFM) for it, there are utilities out there to allow<br>you do this. 1	If you have only one printerport <br>( IRQ7 ), you can change com3 to IRQ5 ( normally 2nd printer ).	<br>Yes, you can change the IRQ's for com3/4, but it depends on your other<br>hardware. com1 uses IRQ4, com2 IRQ3.  If you have only one printerport <br>( IRQ7 ), you can change com3 to IRQ5 ( normally 2nd printer ). For com4,<br>you can assign IRQ2, if its free. 1	disk<br> controller (I6) and keyboard (I1).	 "These LPT1, COM1, disk controller are call devices.  There are<br> devices that requires exclusive interrupt ownership, eg. disk<br> controller (I6) and keyboard (I1).  There are also devices that<br> does not require exclusive ownership, ie. it will share an<br> interrupt with another device, eg. LPT1"<br><br>No.  In a standard ISA bus, the one that almost all non-laptop PCs use,<br>two separate interface cards cannot share an interrupt.  This is due to<br>a screwup in the bus design.  For example, if your Soundblaster wants<br>to drive interrupt number 7, then it must hold a certain bus wire to 0<br>or 1 at all times, depending on whether or not it wants an interrupt.<br>This precludes letting another card assert interrupt number 7.<br><br>When two or more devices in an ISA bus PC share an interrupt, it's<br>because they're implemented by a single card.

1	Please double-check me on this.	I believe that any VL/EISA/ISA motherboard that uses the HINT chipset<br>is limited to 24-bit EISA DMA (where 'real' EISA DMA is 32-bit).  The<br>HINT EISA DMA has the 16 mb ram addressing limitation of ISA.  For this<br>reason I would pass.  I own one of these (HAWK VL/EISA/ISA) and am look-<br>ing to replace it for exactly this reason.<br><br>Please double-check me on this.  In other words, call the motherboard<br>manufacturer and ask them if the motherboard supports true 32-bit EISA<br>DMA.<br><br>Other than this limitation, the motherboard works quite well (I am using<br>mine with DOS 5, Windows 3.1, and UNIX S5R3.2).  Also with Adaptec 1742a<br>EISA SCSI host adapter.<br>

1	The access time usually means the delay time from falling edge of raw<br>address strobe (RAS) to data bus driven.	I'm sorry if I'm misunderstanding your post, but DRAM *does not* have to<br>be refreshed on *each access cycle*.  So cycle time does *not* have to be<br>twice the access time *because of refresh phase*.<br><br>The access time usually means the delay time from falling edge of raw<br>address strobe (RAS) to data bus driven.<br><br>DRAM access cycle timing chart can be roughly shown as following (some<br>signals are intentionally omitted);<br><br>ADDR --<RA><CA>-------<RA><CA>--------- RA=Raw Address, CA=Column Address<br>RAS  ~~~~\________/~~~~~\________/~~~~~  ~=High, _=Low, -=Floating<br>CAS  ~~~~~~~\_______/~~~~~~\_______/~~~  <..>=driven either H or L<br>DATA ---------<VALID>--------<VALID>---<br>         |-------+------|<br>         |-+--|  |<br>           |     +----------- cycle time<br>           +---- access time (or RAS access time)<br><br>Yes, the cycle time is more than twice as the access time but *not*<br>because of the refresh phase.  The refresh can be done either as a<br>trailing phase of normal access cycle or as an individual cycle.<br><br>[other stuff deleted]<br><br>Ken Nakata

1	[Note that discussion of bugs in applications belong in other<br>  newsgroups, and discussion of bugs in OS/2 betas belong in<br>  comp.os.os2.beta]<br><br><br>How to Vote:<br><br> To cast your vote, fill out the ballot below and e-mail it to me.	<br>Introduction:<br><br> This is the third (and final) Call For Votes (CFV) for the creation<br> of four OS/2 newsgroups and the renaming of one:<br> (a)  create comp.os.os2.programmer.porting     (unmoderated)<br> (b)  renaming of comp.os.os2.programmer to<br>      comp.os.os2.programmer.misc               (unmoderated)<br> (c)  comp.os.os2.setup                         (unmoderated)<br> (d)  comp.os.os2.multimedia                    (unmoderated)<br> (e)  comp.os.os2.bugs                          (unmoderated)<br><br> This is the second attempt at creating comp.os.os2.programmer.porting<br> and comp.os.os2.setup, and renaming comp.os.os2.programmer to<br> comp.os.os2.programmer.misc.  The first attempt failed in the summer<br> of 1992 (the voting deadline was August 31, 1992).  The voting rules<br> state that another attempt for creating newsgroups can be started<br> after a six month waiting period (in this case, it is March 1, 1993).<br> This is the first attempt at creating comp.os.os2.multimedia and<br> comp.os.os2.bugs.<br><br> This document contains:<br> *  the background showing the need for these proposals<br> *  the proposed charters for these proposals<br> *  voting instructions<br> *  a voting ballot<br> *  some example ballots<br> *  the voting rules<br> *  the voting schedule<br> *  the Mass Acknowledgement<br><br><br>Background:<br><br> The creation of two, free, 32-bit compilers for OS/2 2.x (gcc/2<br> and emx/gcc; read comp.os.os2.programmer for details) has spurred<br> a continuing deluge of software ported from UNIX platforms, such<br> as emacs, less, awk, grep, sed, xscheme, ispell, flex, yacc, and<br> much more.  Borland has released its C/C++ compiler for OS/2 2.x,<br> allowing for easier porting of DOS and Windows software.<br><br> Much of the PC hardware and drivers were written for DOS, and<br> later, Windows.  As more people are discovering OS/2 2.x, the<br> number of people asking questions about OS/2's compatibility<br> with their hardware increases, as does the questions on the<br> availability of drivers for their hardware, installation<br> procedures, etc.<br><br> Multimedia is becoming popular.  OS/2 2.0 supports Windows<br> Multimedia Extensions using Win-OS/2 3.0.  Furthermore, IBM is<br> including direct multimedia support in OS/2 starting with version<br> 2.1 (in addition to using Win-OS/2 3.1).<br><br> Any non-trivial software will have bugs -- OS/2 is not exempt,<br> especially since IBM is constantly adding new features to OS/2.<br> So far, IBM has issued system patches and corrective service disks<br> (e.g.  the Service Pak) for free (free from BBSs and ftp sites, or<br> for free plus a small media charge for diskettes -- read<br> comp.os.os2.misc for details).<br><br><br>Proposed Charters:<br><br> (a)  create comp.os.os2.programmer.porting (unmoderated)<br><br>  It will provide a forum for developers of ported software so<br>  as to coordinate efforts, avoid duplication of effort, and<br>  spur additional development.  The group will also cover<br>  topics such as porting from other platforms (such as DOS,<br>  Windows, UNIX, etc), toolkits which aid in program<br>  portability (including porting tools such as Mirrors), and<br>  so forth.<br><br> (b)  rename: comp.os.os2.programmer.misc (unmoderated)<br><br>  To keep the structure of the OS/2 newsgroup heirarchy<br>  orthogonal, comp.os.os2.programmer should be renamed to<br>  comp.os.os2.programmer.misc.<br><br>  comp.os.os2.programmer.misc will still be the newsgroup<br>  for OS/2 programmers to discuss programming issues and<br>  technical aspects of OS/2 in an unmoderated setting.<br><br> (c)  create comp.os.os2.setup (unmoderated)<br><br>  It will be devoted to OS/2 system setup topics, including the<br>  availability of device drivers, compatibility information,<br>  installation procedures, system requirements, and overall<br>  performance optimization.<br><br> (d)  create comp.os.os2.multimedia (unmoderated)<br><br>  It will provide a forum for discussion of multi-media issues.<br><br> (e)  create comp.os.os2.bugs (unmoderated)<br><br>  It will provide a forum for OS/2 system bug reports, bug<br>  diagnosis and work arounds, the availability of system<br>  patches and corrective service disks, and so forth.<br><br>  [Note that discussion of bugs in applications belong in other<br>  newsgroups, and discussion of bugs in OS/2 betas belong in<br>  comp.os.os2.beta]<br><br><br>How to Vote:<br><br> To cast your vote, fill out the ballot below and e-mail it to me.<br> Many newsreaders will allow e-mail to be sent by replying to this<br> post.  Be sure to send only the ballot, and edit out the rest of<br> this post.<br><br> 1)  Type in your vote for each proposal:<br><br>     If you favor the charter as proposed, put a "yes" after its name.<br>     If you oppose the charter as proposed, put a "no" after its name.<br>     To abstain, leave a blank after its name.<br><br> 2)  Type in your last name (i.e. your family name), a comma, and<br>     your first name (i.e. your personal name).<br><br> 3)  Cut out the ballot,  Please do not delete any lines of the<br>     ballot.<br><br> 4)  E-mail your ballot to mlevis@lonestar.utsa.edu before 11:59:59 pm<br>     (Central Time), April 24, 1993.<br><br><br>Ballot:<br><br>----------------cut here----------------cut here----------------cut here----<br><br>        (a) comp.os.os2.programmer.porting:<br>        (b)    comp.os.os2.programmer.misc:<br>        (c)              comp.os.os2.setup:<br>        (d)         comp.os.os2.multimedia:<br>        (e)               comp.os.os2.bugs:<br><br>        (f)       voter's last, first name:<br><br>        e-mail ballot to mlevis@lonestar.utsa.edu by April 24<br><br>----cut here----------------cut here----------------cut here----------------<br><br><br>Example Ballot #1:<br><br> (a) comp.os.os2.programmer.porting: yes<br> (b)    comp.os.os2.programmer.misc: yes<br> (c)              comp.os.os2.setup: no<br> (d)         comp.os.os2.multimedia:<br> (e)               comp.os.os2.bugs: no<br><br> (f)       voter's last, first name: Smith, John<br><br> In this example, John Smith favors comp.os.os2.programmer.porting<br> to be created, and comp.os.os2.programmer to be renamed to<br> comp.os.os2.programmer.misc.  He also opposes the creation of<br> comp.os.os2.setup and comp.os.os2.bugs.  He does not have a view<br> on the creation of comp.os.os2.multimedia. <br><br><br>Example Ballot #2:<br><br> (a) comp.os.os2.programmer.porting: yes<br> (b)    comp.os.os2.programmer.misc: yes<br> (c)              comp.os.os2.setup: yes<br> (d)         comp.os.os2.multimedia: yes<br> (e)               comp.os.os2.bugs: yes<br><br> (f)       voter's last, first name: Doe, Jane<br><br> In this example, Jane Doe favors the creation or rename of all<br> the proposals.<br><br><br>Voting Rules:<br><br> * One vote per person.  If you vote more than once, only the most<br>   recent vote will be counted.<br><br> * Votes must be mailed to me by the person voting.  Proxy voting,<br>   forwarding, posting votes to a newsgroup, etc. will not be counted.<br><br> * Do not ask how the votes are going.  The status of the votings<br>   will be revealed only after the poll closes.<br><br> * I will acknowledge votes by Mass Acknowledgement (ACK).  I will<br>   post the ACK twice (see Schedule below).<br><br> * If you need help for using your editor, using e-mail, how<br>   voting works in general, etc. then ask an expert at your site.<br>   Also see the ``How To Create a New Newsgroup'' article which is<br>   posted to news.answers on a regular basis.<br><br> * If you need any clarifications on voting procedures for this<br>   CFV, send me e-mail at mlevis@ringer.cs.utsa.edu.<br><br> * When the voting period is over (see Schedule below), a proposal<br>   passes if both of the following formulas are true:<br>   1)  the number of YES votes exceeds the number of NO votes<br>       by at least 100 (i.e. YES >= NO + 100, or YES - NO >= 100).<br>   2)  the number of YES votes exceeds at least twice the<br>       number of NO votes (i.e. YES >= 2 * NO, or YES - NO >= NO).<br>   In other words, a proposal passes if:<br>       YES - NO >= max (100, NO)<br>   where max() returns the highest number given to it.<br><br><br>Schedule:<br><br> The voting period started on March 29 when the first CFV was posted<br> by David Lawrence (the news.announce.newgroups moderator).<br><br> This third CFV is a repeat of the first CFV, but it also has the<br> Mass Acknowledgement (ACK) of names and e-mail addresses of those who<br> have already voted -- re-send your vote if it is not there.  If you<br> have not voted yet, vote now!<br><br> The voting period will end at 11:59:59 pm (Central Time), on<br> April 24, 1993.  Votes received after that time will not count.<br> The voting results and tally will be posted shortly after that<br> date.<br><br><br>Mass Acknowledgement:<br><br> Here is the list of people who have 1	Note that doing so exposes you to intermittent<br>disturbance from interrupts, so you may want to read (say) twice in a<br>row and keep the lower results.	If you only do read/print then there is no reason for the joystick stuff<br>not to take 90% of the time even if it is efficient.<br><br><br>The truth is that int 15H joystick reading IS slow. I read it directly<br>from the hardware port. Note that doing so exposes you to intermittent<br>disturbance from interrupts, so you may want to read (say) twice in a<br>row and keep the lower results. Don't just turn interrupts off, it may<br>prove detrimental to the health of any high speed comms and other<br>devices.<br><br>Here is an example of how to do this:<br><br>/* ------------------------------ joy.c ------------------------------------- */<br><br>/* An example of how to read PC joystick port by polling the hardware port<br> * directly.<br> * Uses inp()/outp() for byte port access.<br> * Will timeout when 'int count=0' counts up to zero.<br> * This sample reads one port (A is presented and B is in the comments).<br> * You can read both at once by merging the two, but it will time out<br> * when either joystick is not connected.<br> *<br> * There is no need to optimize this routine since it runs for as long as<br> * the joystick circuitry needs.<br> *<br> * Written by Eyal Lebedinsky (eyal@ise.canberra.edu.au).<br>*/<br><br>#include <stdio.h><br>#include <stdlib.h><br>#include <conio.h><br><br>extern int readjoy (int *x, int *y, int *b1, int *b2);<br><br>#define JOYPORT 0x201<br><br>#define XJOY 0x01 /* for joystick B use 0x04 */<br>#define YJOY 0x02 /* for joystick B use 0x08 */<br>#define XYMASK (XJOY|YJOY)<br>#define BUT1 0x10 /* for joystick B use 0x40 */<br>#define BUT2 0x20 /* for joystick B use 0x80 */<br><br>int     /* returns 0 if ok */<br>readjoy (int *x, int *y, int *b1, int *b2)<br>{<br> register int count;<br> register char temp, joy;<br><br> temp = (char)XYMASK;<br> count = 0;<br> outp (JOYPORT, 0);  /* set trigger */<br> do {<br>  temp ^= (joy = (char)inp (JOYPORT));<br>  if (temp & XJOY)<br>   *x = count;<br>  if (temp & YJOY)<br>   *y = count;<br> } while (++count && ((temp = joy) & XYMASK));<br> *b1 = !(joy & BUT1);<br> *b2 = !(joy & BUT2);<br><br> return (!count);<br>}<br><br>/* This main() is for demonstration.<br>*/<br><br>int<br>main ()<br>{<br> int rc, x, y, b1, b2;

0	I highly<br>recommend using a proper _soft_ mouse pad, and if you are a clean freak,<br>place it in your drawer every night or when you don't use it to keep<br>the dust off.	Try this:  flip your mouse over, and open up the cover that holds the<br>mouse ball in place.  Remove the ball, and inside you should see<br>(probably) 3 rollers.  If any of the rollers have a tiny layer of<br>caked-on dirt, dust, or otherwise unidentifiable scum on them, carefully<br>scrape it off with a small knife (Xacto works great).  *Be Careful*<br>You do not want to gauge the rollers, just clean off the dirt.<br>Put the ball back in, put the cover on, and there you are.  <br><br>I clean a couple of dozen of these every month here.  Another symptom<br>is that when you move the mouse, it seems to "click" along.  If this<br>doesn't solve your problem, at least you have a clean mouse.  I highly<br>recommend using a proper _soft_ mouse pad, and if you are a clean freak,<br>place it in your drawer every night or when you don't use it to keep<br>the dust off.<br><br>Personally I prefer track balls.  Oh, which brings me to another point:<br>if your mouse or trackball tracks optically, and the sunlight is <br>streaming through the window onto your mouse/trackball, you may notice<br>that it will stop working.  If this happens to you, close the curtains<br>or blinds, or simply shade your pointing device, and see if that helps.<br>

1	Some disks that won't DISKCOPY<br>properly can be readily Teledisk'd into the proper format...<br>At least this is a software solution for a hardware/BIOS <br>deficiency, eh?	Another (albeit strange) option is using a program like 800 II<br>(available via anonymous FTP at many major sites), or FDFORMAT<br>(also available via anonymous FTP), that allows you to format<br>5.25HD disks to 1.44Meg, or 3.5"HD disks to 1.2Meg (along with<br>many MANY other formats!) so you can DISKCOPY (yes, the broken<br>MeSsy-DOS DISKCOPY!) the 5.25" disks onto 3.5" disks or vice<br>versa...  I use this techniques with "NON-DOS" self-booting <br>game disks on my old Tandy 1000, and it works...  Another program<br>named Teledisk (shareware--available on many major BBS's) will<br>also make the weird format disks, provided you have 800 II<br>or FDFormat installed.... 0	With windows you can call the OK button ID_OK in every instance,<br>this doesn't work for Motif, you'd have to call it Dialog1_OK,<br>and Motif expects a text string rather than a number.	Just a thought on resources,  It is very important if you do use a<br>multiplatform toolkit to check on how it uses resources.  I have<br>used Glockenspeil commonview under Motif and OS2.  I wrote a resource<br>converter from OS2 to Motif, but it really wasn't too easy, especially<br>the naming scheme. In Motif you cannot rename controls/widgets.<br>With windows you can call the OK button ID_OK in every instance,<br>this doesn't work for Motif, you'd have to call it Dialog1_OK,<br>and Motif expects a text string rather than a number.  So <br>your constructor should know how to convert a #define into the<br>proper resource identifier.<br>I'd check on how the toolkit expects names, and that if it does<br>use resources, that is uses resources for all platforms you intend to<br>port to. ( By the way, I would never use CommonView or Glockenspiel<br>for anything ) <br><br><br><br>Alex.<br><br>

1	Again, this has   <br>nothing to do with SCSI.	IDE also uses DMA techniques.  I believe floppy controller also uses DMA,<br>and most A/D boards also use DMA. 0	A few comparisons (from Patterson: Reduced Instruction set computers.	The original RISCs had small instruction sets, and simple ones. The<br>idea was that a) every instruction should be completable in a single<br>clock cycle and b) to have no microcode and c) extensive pipelines.<br><br>A few comparisons (from Patterson: Reduced Instruction set computers.<br>CACM V28. 1, 1985):<br><br>CPU  Year Instructions Microcode<br>---  ---- ------------ ---------<br>IBM 370/168 1973 208  420Kb<br>DEC VAX 11/780 1978 303  480Kb<br>IBM 801  1980 120  0<br>UCB RISC 1 1982 39  0<br>Stanford MIPS 1983 55  0<br><br>While researching for the VLSI VAX, DEC discovered that 60% of the VAX<br>microcode is there to support 20% of the instruction set which<br>accounted for a mere 0.2% of all instructions executed. The uVAX 32<br>subsetted the architecture onto a single chip and used a software<br>emulator for these very complex instructions, the full VLSI uVAX<br>included the entire instruction set, was 5-10 times more copmlex but<br>only ranm 20% faster.<br><br>CPU  Chips Microcode Transistors<br>---  ----- --------- -----------<br>uVAX 32  2 64K  101K<br>VLSI uVAX 9 480K  1250K<br>

1	The 'T' connectors are simply tracks with slots on them - no electronics<br>on it.	I'm not sure if this will work, but how about using one of those 'T' expanders<br>used in the low-profile cases.  They allow 3-5 slots staggered on either<br>side of the card.  You can install it in the last slot, and then (probably) <br>have 2 or 3 sideways slots.  This is actually how it is done in the low<br>profile cases - a standard motherboard, the 'T' connector in one slot, <br>and the expansion cards plugged into the 'T'.  I guess you could do this at <br>each end of the slots (1 & 8) to add even more.  <br><br>The 'T' connectors are simply tracks with slots on them - no electronics<br>on it.  The only downside - your case won't close, but for a homebrew system,<br>that may not be a problem.<br><br>Don't know about performance, though.  I'll leave that discussion to the <br>engineers.
